==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The current master thesis concerns the use of recycled concrete aggregates, RCA, as a replacement of natural
aggregates, NA, in the production of concrete. In the building industry the awareness of the necessity of
designing sustainable buildings is continuously growing, and as concrete is the world’s most used building
material it is pertinent to investigate the possibilities of optimizing concrete with regard to sustainability.
The amount of construction and demolition waste, CDW, from the building industry has been steadily
increasing during recent years. CDW has proven to be a potential source of aggregates for concrete, seeing
that the material properties of RCA are to some extent aligned with those of NA.
A characterization of both coarse recycled concrete aggregates, C-RCA, and ﬁne recycled concrete aggre-
gates, F-RCA, is carried out to document the properties of the aggregates in order to create concrete mix
designs with varying replacement ratios of RCA. The experiments conducted are the determination of par-
ticle size distribution, density and water absorption, water content, and cement content, after which ﬁve
concrete mixes are investigated with respect to workability, air content and compressive strength. Here it
is found that the properties of the C-RCA are in alignment with NA, whereas the water absorption differs
signiﬁcantly for F-RCA in comparison with NA. Furthermore, it is concluded that it is possible to obtain a
desired mean compressive strength of 20MPa, and that the strength decreases upon an increase of RCA.
To investigate the yield condition derived for beams casted with F-RCA and C-RCA, nine beams are casted
using the concrete mix design F-RCA50. The concrete mix F-RCA50 has an aggregate distribution of 60%
coarse aggregates and 40% ﬁne aggregates, where the entire coarse fraction is C-RCA and the ﬁne fraction
is 50% F-RCA and 50% NA. In reference to the beams casted with RCA three beams are casted using
the reference mix design F-NA40, where the aggregate distribution is 60% coarse NA and 40% ﬁne NA.
The beams are tested in three different test conﬁgurations, where the beams are subjected to a combination
of bending, shear, and torsion. In conclusion the beams in all conﬁgurations exhibited a similar material
behavior in alignment with traditional concrete and well-known theory, which indicates that RCA is a
suitable alternative to NA.
Furthermore, the environmental impacts of concrete casted with RCA compared to traditional concrete is
quantiﬁed through a life cycle assessment, LCA. The LCA is simpliﬁed to solely consider the production
stage of the concrete, and results in a decrease of 2% in global warming potential, GWP, between F-NA40
and F-RCA50.

Preface
This master thesis was carried out at the Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of
Denmark in cooperation with NIRAS A/S. The work was ﬁnalized during the spring semester of 2022 and
serves as the completion of the MSc in Architectural Engineering. During the project work both authors
have contributed equally to the experimental work and the current report corresponding to 30 ECTS credits.
Additionally, Jelena Kuburovic is responsible for an Annex E corresponding to further 5 ECTS credits.
For the experimental work crushed concrete aggregates were kindly provided by Norrecco A/S, cement by
Aalborg Portland A/S, and remaining materials by the Department of Civil Engineering at DTU, and for
this the authors would like to express their appreciation.
Regarding the characterization of the crushed concrete aggregates thanks are due to Ebba Cederberg Schnell
and to Ditte Jul Valentin. In addition the authors want to express sincere gratitude for all help and encour-
agement received by the laboratory staff, especially to Kristian Riskær, Klaus Bræmer, Jørn Lykke Jensen,
Christian Rasmussen, and Troels Kristensen. Without your valuable input and willingness to help the ex-
perimental work would not have been successful. Furthermore, gratitude is due to Ian Rasmussen, Michael
Ramskov, and Tim Larsen for assistance throughout the experimental work.
Finally, the authors would like to sincerely thank Lars Zenke Pørlov Hansen from NIRAS A/S for fre-
quent meetings, valuable input, and his interest and availability to the project, which is greatly appreciated.
Additionally, acknowledgement is owed to external lecturer at the Department of Civil Engineering Egil
Borchersen for motivation, guidance, and assistance throughout the project and to PhD Student Louise
Green Pedersen for her input on crushed concrete aggregates.
Cecilie Kristensen & Jelena Kuburovic

Introduction
Worldwide focus on green transition is increasing as governments are implementing solutions that aim
towards reducing the environmental footprint such as carbon footprint and use of non-renewable resources
amongst others. The construction industry is no exception with the world’s increasing population and the
urbanization along with the industrialization of different countries; the demand of structures and buildings
is only increasing [18]. Globally, the construction industry is accountable for approximately 40% of the
world’s yearly carbon emissions, of which 28% are caused by building operations throughout their life
time, and the remaining 12% are related to the mining and production of materials, transport, construction,
and reuse of building materials. In contrast the aviation industry is responsible for about 2-3% of the yearly
global carbon emissions [5].
The largest culprit to the environmental footprint arising from waste generated by demolition of buildings
and infrastructure is concrete, which is sensible seeing that concrete is the world’s most commonly used
building material as it can be locally sourced almost anywhere since it consists of water, cement, and ag-
gregates [31]. Other factors that add to the widespread use of concrete are the functionality, aesthetics, low
energy consumption, and durability of concrete, which all contribute to concrete often being an economi-
cally sound option for structural purposes [3]. Studies show that the two main contributors to the carbon
emission of buildings are to be found in the erection and the operation respectively, and during recent years
the erection of buildings tend to increase its share of the total carbon emission, which is due to optimisations
of energy resources and transitioning into green energy options [28]. Thus, it is now more than ever relevant
to take the mining of raw products, production of structural materials, and the construction of buildings into
consideration from an environmental perspective.
Today, the European building industry is responsible for the generation of approximately 850 million tonnes
of construction and demolition waste, CDW, per year, which corresponds to 31% of the total waste gen-
eration i Europe [25]. In Denmark a similar tendency is observed, however it is slightly increased as the
construction industry is accountable for 35% of the total waste generation [7], where up to 25% are directly
related to concrete [29]. The total yearly amount of CDW in Denmark is not known, however it is assessed
to be around 2 million tonnes, which is handled either by being disposed in landﬁlls causing environmental
pollution or alternatively being sorted and crushed and then used to replace aggregates in road ﬁll or in
new concrete [29]. With increasing environmental challenges it is relevant to assess whether the building
1
==PAGE:18==
industry can make better use of the generated CDW. Seeing that the largest amount is related to concrete
it is pertinent to investigate the opportunity of increasing the use of CDW in new concrete by crushing
the concrete waste into aggregates resulting in recyclable concrete aggregates. The recycled concrete ag-
gregates, RCA, are divided into a coarse and a ﬁne fraction which makes the aggregates applicable as an
alternative to natural aggregates, NA. Upon replacing NA with RCA in structural concrete it is important
to obtain knowledge about the properties that characterize the aggregates as this is essential in creating mix
designs that comply with given requirements of the building industry. Similarly, it is paramount to obtain
information of the behaviour of structural members casted with recycled concrete in the ultimate limit state.
1.1
Problem Statement
Currently, the design codes allow replacement ratios of NA with RCA depending on the knowledge of the
RCA and the exposure class of the new concrete. For normal strength concrete these replacement ratios are
given as 10% and 20% for the ﬁne and coarse fractions of NA respectively, and a number of general and
supplementary requirements need to be documented to carry out the replacement. Upon wishing to increase
these replacement ratios further documentation of the material properties of the RCA is required [12]. The
purpose of this master thesis is to investigate the inﬂuence of replacing larger ratios of NA with RCA in the
production of new concrete than what is permitted during design today. Consequently, the current thesis
is based on experimental work combined with literature studies and calculations based on plasticity theory
due to reinforcement and concrete being considered ideally plastic materials.
As the interest and demand of sustainability in the building industry are continuously growing it is important
to examine alternatives to traditional concrete. Hence, this project aims to document the yield condition
for concrete beams casted with ﬁne and coarse crushed concrete aggregates, denoted F-RCA and C-RCA
throughout the thesis, where the following research questions will be examined:
• What inﬂuence does the amount of replaced F-RCA have on the material properties of concrete?
• How does the behavior of beams casted with F-RCA and C-RCA differentiate from the behavior of
beams casted with traditional concrete?
• Is the yield condition for traditional concrete and thereby the well known calculation methods appli-
cable for beams casted with F-RCA and C-RCA?
• What inﬂuence does the use of RCA have on the overall environmental impact of structural concrete?
2
==PAGE:19==
1.1.1
Delimitation
This thesis is limited to RCA that originate from CDW, whereas usally RCA also cover the surplus from
concrete productions both on site and from concrete obtained in precasting processes. In the work with
the concrete mixes the current thesis focuses solely on the replacement of NA with RCA, and the use of
additives such as ﬂy ash, microsilica, and plasticizer is disregarded. Furthermore, the thesis is limited to
only include the passive environmental class of concrete as this is commonly used for structures above
ground in the industry. Additionally, the focus is on beam behaviour in the ultimate limit state, and thereby
not considering the serviceability limit state. Concrete behavior related to creep and shrinkage is also
disregarded.
1.2
Structure of Report
As this report aims to document the yield condition for concrete beams casted with F-RCA and C-RCA,
it is based on literature studies, theoretical considerations, and experiments with concrete cylinders and
beams. Therefore, a great extent of the project work carried out is experimental, and for the sake of clarity
the results of the experiments are collected in annexes belonging to the current report. Additionally, the life
cycle assessment that forms the basis of evaluating the sustainability of the concrete mixes appears in an
independent annex. Thus, the structure of the report is shown in Figure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Structure of report
3
==PAGE:20==
The section of theory provides relevant information on concrete, reinforcement, plasticity theory, and com-
bined impacts of beams resulting in a theoretical yield condition. Next, the methods that form the basis
of the project work are brieﬂy described. The results section consists of an analysis of the experimental
work executed in Annexes B and C, where the contents of Annex A are used in the data processing of
Annexes B and C. The experimental work in Annex B contains a series of experiments which constitutes
the characterization of the crushed concrete aggregates and thereby forms the basis of the cylinder testing.
Similarly, Annex C contains a series of beam experiments and the determination of the yield strength of
reinforcement. Additionally, the work is compared to theoretical considerations and further elaborated by a
comparison with test results of experiments carried out in other projects. Furthermore, the sustainability of
the recycled concrete is evaluated in comparison with regular concrete, which is presented in Annex D and
further elaborated in the main report. All ﬁndings are discussed in a larger context related to the objective
of increasing sustainability of concrete in the building industry. Finally, a conclusion will present answers
to the research questions stated above.
Annex E corresponds to 5 ECTS credits belonging to Jelena Kuburovic, which consists of a further investi-
gation of the results found in Annex B.
4
==PAGE:21==
2
|
State of the Art
The theory of beams with regard to bending and shear is often credited to Bernoulli and Euler for their
works on ﬂexural strength and stiffness, and additionally both Galileo, Coulomb, and several others have
contributed to what is known as beam theory today. Furthermore, Coulomb performed some of the ﬁrst
well known studies on torsion, which were later described and enhanced by Timoshenko and Saint-Venant
amongst others [20]. Thus stating that theoretical considerations regarding the impacts beams may be
subjected to have been developed throughout several hundred years with contributions from all over the
world.
The behavior of structural members is governed by the impacts that the members are subjected to, and often
different impacts occur simultaneously, which can be detrimental for a structure if not taken into account.
Previous studies regarding combined impacts of reinforced concrete structures have been carried out in
Sweden [20] and USA [30] amongst others, and the superiority of these studies are based on the theory of
plasticity.
During the past decade several researchers and scientists have investigated the possibilities of using ag-
gregates from construction demolition waste, CDW, as a replacement of natural aggregates, NA, in new
concrete, and in this regard it is relevant to examine both the geometrical and mechanical properties of
crushed concrete aggregates. Extensive research projects have been carried out all around the world, and
countries such as China [17], the Netherlands [15], Serbia [25] and Spain [23] have contributed to the ﬁnd-
ings of structural behavior of concrete casted with recycled concrete aggregates, RCA. In general current
research regarding the subject of RCA has primarily focused on the coarse fraction, and the possibilities
of using coarse recycled concrete aggregates, C-RCA, as an alternative to the natural coarse fraction. The
ﬁndings showed no signiﬁcant change in compressive strength when replacing up to 30% of the coarse
natural aggregates, whereas the strength is decreasing with a largely increasing amount of C-RCA [9]. An
investigation showed that the largest decrease was found to be 7.2% which corresponds to a replacement of
100% of the coarse fraction, found for a concrete aiming for 25MPa and a water to cement ratio of 0.55[22].
The possibility of using the ﬁne fraction of the recycled concrete aggregates as an alternative is investigated
to a lesser extent, seeing that the ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA, may cause shrinkage effects
5
==PAGE:22==
and contain large amounts of attached mortar [24]. Also, the high water absorption of the ﬁne fraction of
recycled concrete aggregates may result in a more permeable cement paste [23], which can jeopardize the
mechanical properties of the new concrete.
In a variety of the research articles published on the subject of RCA the structural behaviour of beams is
investigated when casted with varying amounts of RCA. Amongst others M. Exteberria, A. R. Marí, and E.
Vázquez have examined full scale beams exposed to shear failure and found that the inﬂuence of replacing
NA with RCA is only minor when looking at the coarse fraction of aggregates, and that the failure loads
are similar for the beams tested, which include a reference beam casted with only natural aggregates and
three different concrete mixes with varying contents of RCA being 25%, 50%, and 100% respectively [23].
However, despite the inﬂuence of increasing the amount of C-RCA is minor, it still causes the compressive
strength of the concrete to slightly decrease. From the literature reviewed in this regard it is found that
regardless of bending and shear being investigated in different series of experiments, the combination of
impacts exposed to beams casted with RCA is not evaluated.
Besides from the research and experiments carried out actual construction projects have successfully been
executed in countries such as China [17] and Denmark [19]. In 2010 in Shanghai, China, a four storey
building named the Eco-House was made with recycled concrete, where the total amount of coarse aggre-
gates were replaced with RCA with compressive strengths of 30MPa and 40MPa. Furthermore, at Prags
Boulevard in Amager, Copenhagen, a demonstration project was carried out in 2015 for the Pelican Self
Storage building in coorporation between Pelcon A/S and Lendager. Here, RCA was used for on site casting
of ground slabs and walls, where it was avoided adding extra cement to the concrete mix, thus exemplifying
that using RCA in structural concrete does not necessarily result in an increase of the cement content seeing
that the incorporation of ﬂy ash into the concrete mix design was sufﬁcient in this case.
Based on the literature reviews the following observations are made; C-RCA is investigated and proved
applicable in new concrete to a certain extent, whereas F-RCA has shown some disadvantageous properties,
and furthermore experiments on beams subjected to combined impacts are limited. Therefore, the current
project contributes to the investigations and the lack of knowledge on certain aspects by considering the
C-RCA as a suitable alternative to the coarse NA. Additionally, the F-RCA fraction is investigated by
replacing the ﬁne NA with different amounts of F-RCA. Furthermore the structural properties of beams
casted with recycled concrete aggregates are investigated when subjected to combined impacts of bending,
shear, and torsion. Finally, the environmental impact related to the processes of extracting and producing
NA is compared to the processes found for RCA.
6
==PAGE:23==
3
|
Theory
The current chapter consists of a theoretical description of the materials concrete and steel, where the
composition of concrete and the stress-strain relations for both concrete and steel are elaborated. This is
followed by an elaboration of the theory of plasticity, including Coulomb’s failure hypothesis, the upper
and lower bound theorems, and the uniqueness theorem. Finally, the theory behind bending, shear, torsion
and the combination of these impacts is presented.
3.1
Concrete
Concrete in its simplest form consists of cement, water, and aggregates. The most widely used cement is
Portland Cement, which consists of a variety of types such as Basis Cement, Rapid Cement and Futurecem
[2]. The cement is a dry powdered substance that hardens when reacting with water. The water and the
cement compose the cement paste, which works as a binder of the aggregates and thereby forms concrete.
The aggregates consist of different sized sand and gravel, which are usually dredged from pits, rivers or the
seabed, thereby the name natural aggregates. These aggregates are divided into two fractions namely the ﬁne
and the coarse fractions, which correspond to sand and gravel respectively. The ﬁne fraction represents grain
diameters of 0-4mm, where the coarse fraction covers the range from 4-32mm. The aggregate constitutes
the largest part of the concrete composition, with a percentage usually around 65-75% of the total mass. [8]
The composition of the different components has a great inﬂuence on the properties of the concrete and
is dependent on the purpose of the speciﬁc concrete. The distribution of the ﬁne and the coarse fractions
inﬂuences the workability of fresh concrete. A concrete mixture consisting of mainly the ﬁne fraction
results in a dry consistency which reduces the workability of the concrete. Contrarily, concrete consisting
of a great amount of the coarse fraction will make the concrete less homogeneous, due to the voids between
the gravel and the cement paste. The ratio between the ﬁne and the coarse fractions along with the grading
of the aggregates are therefore important parameters to consider when designing a concrete mix. The
aggregate grading, which is determined by the particle size distribution of the aggregates, ensures that the
aggregates are packed sufﬁciently and thereby form a homogeneous concrete where the smaller particles
ﬁll the voids around the larger grains. [31]
7
==PAGE:24==
Other factors of inﬂuence for the concrete composition is the density of the aggregates. A concrete mixture
is made based on the masses of the different components, where the density of the aggregates is relevant for
the determination of how much the aggregates constitute of the total mass of the concrete. Another factor
is the aggregates’ ability to absorb water, and depending on the state of the aggregates, the aggregates may
absorb water from the concrete mixture. If the aggregates are very dry the particles will absorb the water
that should be reacting with the cement and work as the binder, which will affect the water to cement ratio.
The water to cement ratio has a crucial impact on the compressive strength of the concrete. A low water to
cement ratio leads to a stronger concrete, whereas a higher ratio leads to a lower compressive strength [31].
The increased water content induces a porous concrete, due to the excess water evaporating over time and
leaving air pores in the concrete, the air pores thereby reducing the strength of the concrete [6].
3.1.1
Compressive Strength
The compressive strength is commonly used to characterize concrete and can be determined by uniaxial
compression tests of cylinders, where the characteristic strength, denoted fc, is found. The usual relation
between stresses and strains in concrete is shown in Figure 3.1, where the compressive strain is shown
positive. The relation applies for normal strength concrete, which spans from 12-50MPa, whereas high
strength concrete is determined by compressive strengths larger than 50MPa [6].
Figure 3.1: Stress-strain relation for concrete [11]
From the ﬁgure above it is apparent that the stress-strain relation is linear for small stresses, where the
concrete behaves elastically and the displacements are reversible. The relation becomes nonlinear as the
stresses increase, after which the displacements become irreversible. The peak is found where the maximum
stress is reached which corresponds to the compressive strength of the concrete, fc. At the peak stress the
8
==PAGE:25==
strain is denoted "c, and after this point failure will occur where the yield strain, denoted "cu is found.
According to the current standard a theoretical value of 2h is chosen as the strain corresponding to the
maximum compressive stress, whereas the yield strain is 3.5h [11].
Thus, concrete is characterized by the pronounced formable and durable properties, and a great compressive
strength, which makes concrete useful in many different constellations, these are some of the advantages
of concrete. However, certain limitations of concrete are found in its low tensile strength and low ductility,
which is the reason for introducing reinforcement in the concrete when considering concrete as a structural
material.
3.1.2
Reinforcement steel
Upon combining concrete and reinforcement a structural material with both good compressive and tensile
strengths is obtained. One of the advantages of combining reinforcement and concrete is that both materials
expand and contract by approximately equal amounts during temperature ﬂuctuations [13]. This allows for
an adequate bond to be established between the reinforcement and the concrete, thus acting like a composite
material, an effect which is only ampliﬁed with the use of ribbed bars and stirrups. The yield strength, fy,
of the reinforcement is the resistance to failure by deformation, whereas the tensile strength, fu, is the
resistance to failure by fracture. Reinforcement steel is divided into three ductility classes, A, B, and C, in
which class A is low-ductility steel, class B is medium-ductility steel, and class C is high-ductility steel.
For structural steels in ductility grade B a ratio between the yield strength and the tensile strength of 1.08 is
deemed satisfactory according to current standards [11], which secures adequate ductility of the reinforced
concrete.
Figure 3.2: Stress-strain relation of hot-rolled reinforcement steel [11]
9
==PAGE:26==
The ratio between the yield and tensile strengths is apparent from Figure 3.2 above, where the actual stress-
strain relation for hot-rolled reinforcement steel is shown as well as the ideal-plastic relation commonly used
for calculations within the theory of plasticity. The yield strength of reinforcement steel is determined from
the stress-strain relation curve created upon a tensile test of the reinforcement and it is deﬁned as the largest
stress prior to the ﬁrst decrease in stress. Until the stresses reach this point reinforcement steel usually
exhibits a linear relation between stress and displacement. The yield strength, fy, will ﬂuctuate between an
upper and a lower yield strength before the stress-strain relation curve will have another peak due to strain
hardening of the steel. When the deformations become plastic they are permanent due to dislocations being
introduced in the steel, which cause ﬂaws to occur in the crystalline structure. These ﬂaws contribute to
an increase in the yield stress since additional stresses are needed to move the dislocations further. Thus,
strain hardening is the increase in stress that is required to cause an increase in strain in a material that
is deforming plastically. This next peak value will occur prior to failure and is known to be the ultimate
tensile strength, fu, of the reinforcement. The strain at which ultimate tensile strength occurs is known as "u.
Eventually, due to necking the stress will decrease and failure will occur, and at this point the reinforcement
no longer has structural capacity [1].
3.2
Theory of Plasticity
Throughout the current report the reinforced concrete beams are treated based on the theory of plasticity,
thus assuming the reinforced concrete to behave as a rigid plastic material. A yield condition of a material
is an expression of the combination of stresses that allow the material to yield. For a material to yield the
strain is increased without an increase in stress, and therefore the theory of plasticity deals with materials
that can deform plastically when the load becomes sufﬁciently high. Thus, plasticity is a time independent
material, non-linear phenomenon. [27]
Figure 3.3: Idealized uniaxial stress-strain relation
10
==PAGE:27==
In reality rigid plastic materials do not exist, however, the theory of plasticity is considered applicable for
materials where the plastic strain is substantially larger than the elastic strain. Such an idealisation of rigid-
plastic behaviour is depicted in Figure 3.3. The rigid-plastic model is an idealisation of reality due to elastic
strains occurring before the yield stress is reached. Plastic deformations will eventually transpire in parts
of a structure affected by a load, and generally a further increase of load is possible as the stresses are able
to increase in the remaining parts of the structure. Upon reaching the yield stress in a sufﬁciently large
part of the structure the load carrying capacity is exhausted seeing that strains will then increase without an
increase in stresses. [27]
As concrete is usually regarded a brittle material it is to some extent unﬁt for plasticity analysis, and the
ductile properties of reinforcement steel is the main reason that reinforced concrete can be considered a
plastic material. Making use of the plastic properties of reinforced concrete is not a new phenomenon as
this goes back to the beginning of the 20th century [27], and this is practical due to the ability to predict
failure of a structural element. Continuous loading of such an element will eventually lead to failure when
the material in question is no longer able to withstand the stresses that occur as a consequence of the loading.
However, crack growth will indicate an upcoming failure, and this is desirable as it allows for precautions
to be taken.
For calculations within the theory of plasticity it is therefore a requirement that the material considered has
sufﬁcient plastic properties, and thus the assumption that the reinforced concrete behaves as a rigid plastic
material is the basis of calculations carried out in the current report. In order to apply the theory of plasticity
the behavior of concrete is assumed ideal-plastic, and since concrete has poor plastic properties a correction
factor is introduced.
Figure 3.4: Comparison between ideal-plastic and real uniaxial stress-strain relation [27]
The correction factor is also called the effectiveness factor, ⌫, and is determined by comparing the real
uniaxial stress-strain relation found by tests and the ideal-plastic stress-strain curve. In Figure 3.4 both
11
==PAGE:28==
curves are illustrated, where the black curve depicts the real stress-strain relation and the red curve depicts
the ideal-plastic relation. The effectiveness factor is determined by implying that the areas under the curves
are equivalent, and the correction of the compressive strength has shown a good correlation between tests
and plastic calculations [4]. The expression of the effectiveness factor differs depending on the different
impacts bending, shear, and torsion respectively, where the expressions are given below [6]
⌫m = 0.98 −fc
500
(3.1)
⌫v = 0.7 −fc
200
(3.2)
⌫t = 0.7(0.7 −fc
200)
(3.3)
3.2.1
Coulomb’s Failure Hypothesis
Coulomb’s frictional hypothesis is developed from an observation that friction and cohesion affect failure
occurring along a yield plane, which is sensible seeing that friction is the force arising between sliding sur-
faces and therefore works in the direction opposite to the movement of the yield plane. Similarly, cohesion
is determined by the structure of the molecules of the material considered, as the cohesion is the inter-
molecular force that binds particles together. Upon observing failure along a yield plane the relative motion
between the particles of the failure surface is assumed parallel to the failure surface, which is known as
sliding [27]. Therefore, the frictional hypothesis expresses a combination of shear and normal stresses that
will cause failure of a material as yielding will arise when the shear stresses exceed the sliding resistance
given by
|⌧| = c −µσ
(3.4)
⌧

c
is the cohesion
µ
is the coefﬁcient of friction
σ
is the normal stress counted positive as a tensile stress
For a Coulomb material exposed to pure compression it can be shown that failure planes may form an angle
of 45° −1/2 ' parallel with the axial force as apparent from Figure 3.5, where ' is the angle of friction.
[27]

==PAGE:29==
Figure 3.5: Cylinder specimen exposed to pure compression [27]
Additionally, when observing the yield condition of a Coulomb material depicted on a stress-shear curve the
stresses meet in a point corresponding to zero strain, and the angle in between these lines is corresponding
to two times the angle of friction. Thus, knowing the coefﬁcient of friction in the relation above the angle
of friction is determined as shown below

3.2.2
The Modiﬁed Coulomb’s Failure Hypothesis
Upon application of Coulomb’s failure hypothesis to concrete too large uniaxial tensile strength is obtained,
and therefore the application of a tension cutoff results in the modiﬁed Coulomb’s failure hypothesis, which
is the basis of determining a yield condition in the current project. The tension cutoff is a limitation of the
largest principal stress, and two failure modes are taken into consideration; namely that the uniaxial tensile
strength of concrete is governed by separation failure and that the uniaxial compressive strength of concrete
is governed by sliding failure. [27]
13
==PAGE:30==
Figure 3.6: The modiﬁed Coulomb’s failure hypothesis
Above, Figure 3.6 can be used to determine whether a speciﬁc state of stress will lead to failure and which
kind of failure will occur. The stress state is depicted using principal stresses and Mohr’s circles, which
indicates that a stress state corresponding to the intersection between separation and sliding failure is not
possible to obtain.
3.2.3
The Lower Bound Theorem
The lower bound theorem is used to determine values of a load that is less than or equal to the yield load
of a structural member. Thus, using the lower bound theorem will result in a safe and statically admissible
stress distribution, that satisﬁes the equilibrium conditions and the statical boundary conditions for the load.
This means that a load found by the lower bound theorem will not be able to result in a collapse of the
structure for which the load is determined.
3.2.4
The Upper Bound Theorem
Similarly, the upper bound theorem is used to determine values of a load that is larger than or equal to the
yield load of a structural member by making use of the work equation. While considering an arbitrary ge-
ometrically possible displacement ﬁeld that corresponds to the strains that are possible in accordance with
the geometrical boundary conditions an upper bound value of a load can be determined by requiring the in-
ternal work to be in equilibrium with the external work. Upper bound solutions are often applied in practice
despite being theoretically unsafe, which is due to membrane actions being neglected in most calculation
methods. However, accounting for membrane actions upper bound solutions are often considered to be safe
[27], which is due to the theory of plasticity only taking changes in strain by yielding into account, and
membrane actions are caused by changes in strain before yielding.
14
==PAGE:31==
3.2.5
The Uniqueness Theorem
From the uniqueness theorem it is given that the actual load carrying capacity of a structural member will
be somewhere between the highest lower bound value and the lowest upper bound value as shown in Figure
3.7. Therefore, the uniqueness theorem states that only one load can be determined to which it applies,
that both the stress distribution is statically admissible and corresponding to stresses within or on the yield
surface and that the strains corresponding to these stresses can be determined from a geometrically possible
displacement ﬁeld [27]. Upon meeting these two conditions when the lower and the upper bound theorems
coincide an exact failure load can be determined.
Figure 3.7: Depiction of the uniqueness theorem
3.3
Combined Impacts
The following section aims to determine the load carrying capacity of reinforced concrete beams subjected
to combined moment, shear, and torsion on basis of the theory of plasticity. Equilibrium equations are
derived with contributions from both the concrete and the reinforcement, and it is presumed that the tensile
strength of the concrete is equal to zero. Throughout the following the static system considered applies to
four point bending as shown in Figure 3.8.
Figure 3.8: Static system

3.3.1
Bending
For structural members subjected to pure bending compressive stresses will occur where the load is ap-
plied and tensile stresses on the side opposite of the load, after which cracks will transpire and a failure
mechanism will take form.
For the impact of pure bending a rectangular cross section with both tensile and compressive reinforcement
is considered, and in this case the compressive reinforcement is in tension due to the height of the compres-
sion zone being smaller than the concrete cover of the rebars. The cross section has a height, h, and a width,
b, and the concrete cover is denoted csc and cst depending on which longitudinal reinforcement it refers to.
The distance from the upper part of the cross section to the tensile reinforcement is the effective height,
d, whereas the height of the compression zone is denoted x. The cross sectional areas of the compressive
and tensile reinforcement are denoted Asc and Ast respectively, and the stress and strain distributions of the
cross section considered are apparent from Figure 3.9 below. Additionally, the ﬁgure below presents the
reduced height of the compression zone, λx, where the reduction factor is denoted λ. This is introduced in
order to take the actual stress state of the concrete into account, as this forms a parabola in reality and not
a square as what is used in the derivation below. For normal strength concrete the value of λ is set to 0.8,
which is empirically determined and ought to cause good coherence between the resultants of the parabolic
and squared stress states and the placement of the resultant in each stress state [26].
Figure 3.9: Stress and strain distribution for pure bending

Torsion
Torsion is apparent in structural members subjected to eccentric loads, and the torsional capacity is based

3.3.4

Method
This section describes the considerations that form the basis of how the current project is approached with
regard to the methodology of collecting and analyzing data in the examination of the research questions
stated in Section 1.1.
A substantial part of the current report is based on well-known theory and calculations of structural capacity
with regard to bending, shear, and torsion, and these are to a large extent essential for the conclusions of
the research questions. Comprehensive literature studies are carried out in order to acquire knowledge on
previous observations of beam behaviour, thus allowing for an unbiased comparison between the reference
beams and the beams casted with recycled concrete aggregates.
The scientiﬁc method is an approach of studying things that are measurable and observable, and the purpose
of carrying out experiments is to obtain proof of the hypothesis that concrete casted with recycled concrete
aggregates has a behaviour similar to the behaviour of common concrete. Therefore, these experiments aim
to provide empirical evidence that can be interpreted in relation to the theory of plasticity with regard to
reinforced concrete. Consequently, great effort is put into the description and execution of the experimental
part of the current project, seeing that the results of the tests need to be reproducible and veriﬁable in order
to perceived as scientiﬁc explanations. During the project the yield condition is investigated in order to
determine how the combination of impacts inﬂuence the structural capacity of a beam casted with recycled
concrete aggregates. Afterwards, the observations, evidence, and conclusions are analysed in order to
decrease potential weaknesses of the experiments.
25
==PAGE:42==
5
|
Analysis of Experimental Work
The current section highlights and further investigates the results found from the experimental work ex-
ecuted in the Annexes B and C, as these form the basis of deriving a yield condition for concrete casted
with ﬁne and coarse recycled concrete aggregates. Additionally, the results of experimental work are held in
comparison to the theoretical considerations described in Section 3 with regard to the compressive strengths
of the concrete mixes, the friction angles observed from the compressive tests of cylinders, and the bending,
shear, and torsional capacities that deﬁne the yield condition for beams subjected to combined impacts.
5.1
Characterization of Aggregates and Cylinder Testing
In Annex B a particle size distribution is determined to evaluate the applicability of recycled concrete
aggregates, RCA, versus natural aggregates, NA, in concrete mix designs. Next, physical properties of the
aggregates such as density, water absorption coefﬁcients, and water content at the day of the casting along
with the cement content found in the F-RCA fraction are determined. The investigation of the physical
properties of NA and RCA respectively forms the basis of ﬁve concrete mixes F-NA50, F-NA40, F-RCA50,
F-RCA80, and F-RCA100, that are designed for a compressive strength of 27MPa to accommodate a mean
compressive strength of 20MPa. The design of each concrete mix is described in detail in Annex B, and in
Figure 5.1 an overview of the distribution of NA and RCA in each concrete mix is provided.
Figure 5.1: Overview of mix designs
26
==PAGE:43==
Brieﬂy described the name of each concrete mix is based on the content of the ﬁne fraction of aggregates,
seeing that this is the only varying parameter between the concrete mixes. Thus, they all have the same
water to cement ratio, and for the reference mixes the distribution is named according to the distribution
between ﬁne and coarse aggregates, namely by F-NA50 containing 50% ﬁne NA and by F-NA40 containing
40% ﬁne NA. The distribution of 60% coarse aggregates and 40% ﬁne aggregates provided a concrete with
satisfactory workability, and therefore this distribution is chosen for all mix designs with RCA, resulting in
the RCA mixes being named according to the distribution between ﬁne NA and F-RCA in each mix due
to the entire coarse NA fraction being replaced with RCA. Thus, the ﬁne fractions constitute 40% in each
concrete mix, and these are distributed into 50% ﬁne NA and 50% F-RCA for F-RCA50, 20% ﬁne NA and
80% F-RCA for F-RCA80, and ﬁnally 100% F-RCA for F-RCA100 as apparent from Figure 5.1.
From each mix design four cylinders are casted and tested in uniaxial compression to determine the com-
pressive strength of the concrete mixes after 28 days of curing. Furthermore, during casting the air content
is determined and slump tests for each mix design are carried out to evaluate the workability of the con-
crete and thereby the applicability of the concrete in practical use. Additionally, the friction angle of each
cylinder is compared to the commonly used value of 37°[27], which is usually used for traditional normal
strength concrete. The values of the friction angles found for each concrete mix are rather similar, which
indicates that the material behavior during failure is similar to what is present for traditional concrete. This
implies that the theory and calculation methods are applicable for concrete casted with varying ratios of
RCA. Seeing that the compressive strength of concrete is most wide spread parameter to characterize con-
crete, it is crucial to evaluate when comparing traditional concrete and recycled concrete. Ultimately, the
compressive strength of concrete determines the applicability of the different concrete mixes as a structural
materials within the passive environmental class. Thus, the mean compressive strength of each mix design
as determined in Section 9 in Annex B is shown in Figure 5.2.
Figure 5.2: Mean compressive strength of mix designs after 28 days of curing in water
27
==PAGE:44==
Some of the peculiarities of the results include the fact that the concrete mixes comply with the requirement
of a minimum compressive strength of 20MPa with an exception of F-RCA80 as apparent in Figure 5.2.
It seems rather strange that the replacement of the entire coarse and ﬁne fractions with RCA, F-RCA100,
results in a higher compressive strength than the replacement of 80% of the ﬁne NA fraction with RCA. On
the basis of this observation the mix design F-RCA80 was repeated in order to evaluate whether an error may
have occurred during casting of the cylinders. The repetition indicated that the casting procedure was not
the issue, seeing that the concrete was very wet and heterogeneous, which was an unfortunate discovery as
this stipulates that the issue of F-RCA80 is related to the mix design itself. Presumably, the low compressive
strength of F-RCA80 is the result of the water content in the mix design being too high, however, this does
not explain why the F-RCA100 mix design obtains a higher compressive strength. Therefore, both mix
designs F-RCA80 and F-RCA100 are to be further investigated in Annex E, that will be an independent
addition to the current project.
Based on the results of Annex B it is determined to cast beams using two concrete mixes, namely F-NA40
and F-RCA50 of which F-NA40 will be used in order to provide a reference to the F-RCA50 mixture.
Therefore, the behaviour of the cylinders casted with these mix designs is evaluated against the current
standard DS/EN 1992-1-1 [11], where the stress-strain relations of the two mix designs are compared to the
behaviour of the idealised stress-strain relation for structural concrete as apparent in Figure 5.3. Below, the
stress-strain relations for the concrete mixes F-NA40 and F-RCA50 are the mean values determined from
the data processing carried out in Section 9 Annex B. The corresponding stress-strain curve is determined
using the relation between the compressive stress and the shortening strain, equation 3.14 in DS/EN 1992-
1-1 [11], with the maximum compressive strength of F-NA40 and F-RCA50 respectively as apparent in
Figure 5.2.
(a) F-NA40 in comparison with DS/EN 1992-1-1
(b) F-RCA50 in comparison with DS/EN 1992-1-1
Figure 5.3: Comparison of stress-strain relations with DS/EN 1992-1-1
28
==PAGE:45==
From Figure 5.3 it is observed that the reference concrete mix F-NA40 experiences less strain at the maxi-
mum stress compared to the tendency obtained from DS/EN 1992-1-1, furthermore the peak is found prior
to the prescribed value of 2 h given in DS/EN 1992-1-1. Contrarily, the peak of F-RCA50 is found closer
to the prescribed value, thereby concrete mix F-RCA50 is more aligned to the tendency of DS/EN 1992-1-1.
Furthermore, it is detected that the F-RCA50 has a more plastic behaviour than both F-NA40 and DS/EN
1992-1-1. Looking at F-NA40 in comparison to the standard it is conservative seeing that larger stresses
can be obtained by the F-NA40 mix design than what is the basis of non-linear calculations given in DS/EN
1992-1-1, a tendency which is not present for F-RCA50. However, as F-RCA50 behaves more plastic this
has advantages upon theoretically resulting in a less brittle failure than F-NA40. In general both F-NA40
and F-RCA50 are in rather good correspondence with the theoretical stress-strain relation, which underlines
that the material behaviour of concrete casted with RCA is comparable to that of regular concrete; and po-
tentially even better as it is advantageous that the behaviour becomes increasingly plastic upon increasing
the amount of RCA, which is apparent from Figure B.G.7 in Annex B.
The decision of casting beams with mix design F-NA40 and F-RCA50 is supported by the coherence be-
tween the behaviour of the concrete mixes in relation to DS/EN 1992-1-1, that underlines that the concrete
mixes are theoretically applicable in the building industry, which is further examined during the testing of
beams.
5.2
Strength of Reinforcement and Beam Testing
In Annex C a total of 12 equally reinforced beams are tested, where nine beams are casted with F-RCA50
and three are casted with F-NA40, the beams are tested in three different load conﬁgurations. The properties
of the reinforcement and the beam tests form the basis of the determination of the yield condition derived
for beams casted with RCA. The reinforcement consists of ribbed bars and stirrups that are in accordance
with DS/EN 10080, in ductility class B and have a speciﬁed yield strength of 550MPa. As this yield strength
is a minimum value the actual yield strength and ultimate strength of the reinforcement are determined in
Annex C for the longitudinal reinforcement with a diameter of 8mm. The results are summarized in Table
5.1 below, and are presumed to also be applicable for the reinforcement bars and stirrups with a diameter
of 6mm.

The reinforcement layout of the beams casted with F-NA40 and F-RCA50 respectively results in the beam
cross sections being normally reinforced, which allows for an assumption of yielding of the tensile rebars
along with crushing of the concrete simultaneously. A calculation verifying that the reinforcement layout
is normally reinforced is apparent from Appendix A and the reinforcement layout is apparent from Figure
5.4.
Upon, obtaining a yield condition for beams casted with RCA, a theoretical yield condition is obtained
based on the theoretical load carrying capacities. The investigations are based on prerequisites equal to the
ones found in Annex C, where a simply supported beam subjected to four-point bending is considered with
a width, b, of 125mm and a height, h, of 250mm. The point loads are placed with a distance between the
support and the point load, a, of 400mm. Furthermore, the point loads are placed with an eccentricity of
20mm to induce torsional stresses in the beam. The considered beam along with the geometry is illustrated
in Figure 5.4.
Figure 5.4: Illustration of the considered beam
The theoretical load carrying capacities are determined for a concrete compressive strength of 20MPa, and a
yield strength of the reinforcement of 550MPa. The determined load carrying capacities for bending, shear,
and torsion in the theoretical case are shown in Table 5.2, where the corresponding calculations are to be
found in Appendix B.

level curves can be sustained by the cross section of the beam, whereas the combinations of bending and
shear utilization that fall outside the level curves will theoretically result in failure.

The approach above is used in the further treatment of the test results found in Annex C. In total 12 beams
casted with F-RCA50 and F-NA40 respectively are tested in three different test conﬁgurations, which are
named according to the impacts the speciﬁc beam is subjected to. These impacts are moment and shear for
VM1 and VM2, where the difference is found in the length of the shear span, a. Lastly, test conﬁguration
VMT results in each beam being subjected to a combination of bending, shear, and torsion. In all test
conﬁgurations a simply supported beam is subjected to four-point bending, as shown in Figure 5.4, where
VM1 and VMT have a shear span of 400mm, and a shear span of 600mm is used for VM2. Furthermore,
VMT has an eccentricity, e, of 20mm. Figures 5.6a, 5.6b, and 5.6c show the force-displacement curves
determined in data processing Section 4 in Annex C.
(a) Force-displacement curve for VM1
(b) Force-displacement curve for VM2
(c) Force-displacement curve for VMT
Figure 5.6: Force-Displacement curves found in Section 4.3 in Annex C
32
==PAGE:49==
The curves above show a coherence with the stress-strain relation found for hot-rolled reinforcement steel
as shown in 3.2, where strain hardening is introduced after the yield strength is reached. This tendency can
be transferred to the force-displacement curves found for all test conﬁgurations, where the vertical displace-
ment and the force applied are increasing linearly in the beginning, after which the relation bends followed
by a further increase until failure is reached and the force drops. Some small discrepancies are detected in
the linear increase in the beginning, and these are caused by the initiation of cracks in the concrete. How-
ever, the tendency that the failure of the beams is governed by the failure of reinforcement shows that the
failure strength, fu, is relevant to take into consideration in the further work, where a comparison of the load
carrying capacities is carried out.
Additionally, the curves show that the stiffness of the reference beams casted with F-NA40 is larger than
the stiffness of the beams casted with F-RCA50, which is sensible due to the larger compressive strength of
the F-NA40 concrete mix.
Furthermore, Annex C Table 4.4 contains the failure load of each beam, and these are used to calculate the

the failure strength of the reinforcement in correspondence with the considerations regarding the failure
strength of the reinforcement. The determination of MRd, VRd, and TRd is found in Appendix C using the
yield stress of the reinforcement and Appendix D using the failure stress of the reinforcement.

during failure, it is conﬁrmed that the failure load always results in a combination of utilization of shear
and bending that falls outside the yield condition as shown for beam VM1-B-1 in Figure 5.7. From the

ﬁgure it is also apparent that using the failure strength for the reinforcement results in a smaller deviation
from the theoretical yield condition, which is sensible seeing that rupture of the reinforcement was current
in multiple test specimens. Also, the moment capacity is increased upon being calculated with the failure
strength of the reinforcement, thus resulting in a smaller utilization of moment capacity, which causes the
deviation to decrease. This tendency is observed for all beams tested in the three different conﬁgurations.

In the ﬁgures above the yield condition of each reference beam is plotted against the mean yield condition
determined for the beams casted with RCA, referred to as VM1-B-M and VM2-B-M respectively, and the
utilization at failure is presented for each of the beams tested. It is noticeable that the yield conditions
are coinciding, which is due to to the graphs in Figures 5.8a and 5.8b being normalised. Furthermore it is
apparent from Figure 5.8b that the different beam results are placed relatively close, which indicates that the
beam behavior is similar regardless of beams being casted with F-RCA40 and F-NA50. The tendency is not
as apparent for VM1 and VMT, which indicates a greater deviation between the tested beams. Analogously,
the utilization of shear, bending, and torsion is resulting in a yield condition for conﬁguration VMT as
shown in Figure 5.8c. This yield condition does not begin and end in a utilization of 1 for pure bending
and shear respectively, seeing that the load carrying capacity of the beams is reduced when also taking the
utilization of the torsional capacity into account, which is in coherence with Figure 5.5.
In order to asses the load carrying capacities of the beams against theory a comparison between the theo-
retically determined failure load, PRd, and the actual failure load, P, is carried out. This is shown in Figure
5.9, where P and PRd are plotted as x-values and y-values respectively. Thus, the theoretical and experi-
mental failure loads for each load conﬁguration and thereby each beam are plotted along with the line of
best ﬁt. This results in a scatter plot which is intended to provide an overview of the correlation between
the theoretical values and the actual values found by the beam experiments. Points close to the line of
best ﬁt correspond to higher correlations, whereas points further away from the line are depicting lower
correlations between theory and practice.
Figure 5.9: Scatter plot showing the actual failure load versus the theoretical failure load along with a line of best ﬁt
36
==PAGE:53==
Ideally, the experimental values should be equal to the theoretical values corresponding to a linear corre-
lation, plotted as the black line in Figure 5.9. However, from the ﬁgure it is apparent that all values are
below the line, which indicates that the determined load carrying capacities are conservative. From the ﬁg-
ure it is also apparent that the beams tested in load conﬁguration VM2 are placed closer to the line of best
ﬁt, which indicates that the theoretical calculation methods are corresponding to the experimental observa-
tions. A lower correlation is registered for the beams tested in load conﬁguration VM1 and VMT where the
points are further from the ideal line and more scattered. Below, the Tables 5.6, 5.7, and 5.8 summarize the
load carrying capacities determined for each beam as well as the actual failure load for each beam and the
corresponding utilization degrees.
