The degrees of utilization found in Tables 5.6, 5.7, and 5.8 respectively show that the theoretical load carry-
ing capacities are underestimated, which is an indication of the theoretical calculations being conservative
and thereby on the safe side in regard to failure.
As a veriﬁcation of the determined load carrying capacities the results are compared to a test series of beams
casted with traditional concrete and a series of beams casted with RCA. The comparison does not include the
37
==PAGE:54==
results found for VMT since none of the tests series include torsional moments. Below, Figure 5.10 depicts
the results of the beam tests of conﬁgurations VM1 and VM2 plotted in comparison with experiments of
beams exposed to bending and shear carried out in 1959 by Hjalmar Granholm at Chalmers University
of Technology in Gothenburg, Sweden [16]. In these experiments the beams are normally reinforced and
exposed to four point bending with a shear span of approximately 0.97m, and from this information the
shear force at failure is calculated. As only the moment capacity of H. Granholm’s experiments is given for
the beams, the shear capacity is determined using the compressive strength of the concrete of each beam
and the yield stress of each beam using the same method as the calculation of shear capacity in the current
project. Similarly, the results of beams casted with varying ratios of C-RCA by Miren Exteberria in 2007
are depicted in Figure 5.10. In these experiments the utilization degree in shear is given, and from knowing
the failure loads, the shear span of 1.0m, and the geometry of the beams the moment utilization degree is
calculated.
Figure 5.10: Results of VM1 and VM2 in comparison with tests by H. Granholm and M. Exteberria
Above, it is visible that some of the beams tested by H. Granholm are in alignment with the results of the
experiments carried out in the current project, whereas some are utilized signiﬁcantly more in shear. In
his line of experiments no beams tested have the same reinforcement layout, and due to this it is possible
for H. Granholm to introduce larger variations of the utilization of the beams in bending and shear respec-
tively, than what has been possible due to the circumstances regarding the test set-up of the current project.
Furthermore, it is observed from [16] that some of the reinforcement stirrups in the beams tested have an
inclination that increases the shear capacity, which has not been possible to take into account in Figure 5.10
38
==PAGE:55==
seeing that the inclination of the stirrups is not given in [16]. Had this been considered it is presumable
that the results of H. Granholm’s beam tests would align more closely to the results of the beam tests in
the current project. Furthermore, it is observed from Figure 5.10 that the results of M. Exteberria’s experi-
ments are also in coherence with the results obtained in the current project, however in contradiction to the
remaining results the utilization degrees are signiﬁcantly higher in shear than bending. This is due to the
cross sections being heavily reinforced longitudinally, which results in shear failure due to large bending
capacity.
From the comparison it is apparent that all test results are outside the theoretical yield condition, which
shows that the yield condition is conservative regardless of the test conﬁguration, reinforcement degree and
concrete mixture. This underlines the ﬁnding that beams casted with RCA have similar properties as beams
casted with traditional concrete.
5.2.1
Summary
Regarding the tested beams it is observed that the failure is dependent on yielding of the reinforcement and
not the concrete strength, which indicates that the use of RCA in the current mix design is applicable. This
is emphasized by the properties of the aggregates determined in Annex B, that applies to RCA as aggregates
in both fresh and hardened concrete.
The derived yield condition showed a desirable tendency seeing that the test results are all conservative,
which is further emphasized by the utilization degrees found to be approximately 10-20% higher than the
theoretically determined values. Although the results seem to show a desirable tendency, a more compre-
hensive investigation of the yield condition would have been desirable, seeing that the test conﬁgurations
VM1 and VMT provided almost indistinguishable results due to the limitations in the test set-up with regard
to inducing torsion. Furthermore, it had been beneﬁcial for the experiments if the yoke used to distribute
the loads had been longer as it was not possible to induce shear failure of the beams. Thus, the test facilities
available at DTU were not sufﬁcient to investigate to the yield condition fully.
In the theoretical determination of the capacities of the beams the self weight of beams and the mass
of the devices used to induce a torsional moment in the VMT test conﬁguration are neglected as these
are not considered to have signiﬁcant impact on the ﬁndings of the experiments. Furthermore, during
calculation procedures the presumed stress states that form the basis of determining the capacity of the
beams are considered constant, despite minor errors potentially being introduced by crack formation or
bending deformations as such sources of error are neglected.
39
==PAGE:56==
6
|
Environmental Footprint
The current section elaborates the results obtained from the life cycle assessment, LCA, carried out in Annex
D, that seeks to investigate whether replacing natural aggregates, NA, with recycled concrete aggregates,
RCA, in structural concrete introduces a possibility of reducing the environmental footprint of concrete.
As described in Annex D the environmental footprint of concrete is determined by considering seven dif-
ferent impact categories, where the global warming potential, GWP, is found to have the greatest impact
of all seven categories. GWP is a measure of the footprint of greenhouse gases, which is the most com-
monly used parameter when considering environmental impacts. Based on the observations in Annex D
where GWP constitutes the largest share of the environmental footprint of concrete, solely this parameter is
further investigated in the following section. Upon considering GWP all greenhouses gases are taken into
account and held in relation to carbon dioxide, due to this being the most emitted greenhouse gas in terms
of quantities released and of the total global warming impact [21]. Therefore, in the following the emission
of greenhouse gases is referred to as GWP, which is also commonly known as the carbon footprint.
As the concrete mix design F-RCA50 is the primary focus of the current report due to its material properties
being in good coherence with the reference concrete F-NA40, and seeing that it complies with the functional
unit stated as the basis of the LCA study, it is chosen to only elaborate on the results of F-NA40 and F-
RCA50. Looking at the environmental footprint of these two mix designs a decrease of 2% in GWP is
observed upon replacing NA with RCA. This decrease in GWP is not signiﬁcant, however, when taking the
amounts of concrete yearly produced in the Danish building industry into account, it is still considered a
potential to reduce the environmental footprint of concrete.
Based on the ﬁnding that the reduction of GWP is not large, it is relevant to examine the potential of avoid-
ing extraction of nonrenewable resources such as sand and gravel which constitute 65-75% of structural
concrete. On a yearly basis around 7.3 million m3 natural aggregates are mined from seabeds and excava-
tion pits in Denmark used in concrete for the building industry [7]. It is difﬁcult to determine when danish
natural resources will no longer be available, but with a steadily increasing need of concrete in the building
industry, it is highly relevant to consider the use of RCA as an alternative to NA.
40
==PAGE:57==
7
|
Discussion
In the present thesis the results acquired from the experimental work, which are described in detail in Annex
B and Annex C, are discussed and compared to previous ﬁndings and research from existing literature.
Furthermore, the results of Annex D are used to clarify the possibilities of decreasing the environmental
footprint of structural concrete.
The experimental studies involving a full replacement of coarse recycled concrete aggregates, C-RCA,
and varying replacement ratios of ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA, corroborated the observation
from existing literature that increasing amounts of RCA in structural concrete decreases the compressive
strength of the concrete. However, despite large amounts of both fractions of aggregates being replaced in
the experiments, the mean compressive strength in two of three concrete mixes is still in compliance with the
minimum requirement of 20MPa. As the material behaviour of concrete can vary across batches the industry
commonly uses the 5% fractile to prescribe a certain compressive strength of a concrete mix, and therefore
a substantially larger amount of cylinders had ideally been investigated to obtain better statistical proof of
the claim that the compressive strength of the concrete batches is indeed as desired. Upon considering the
compressive strengths found for the appertaining cylinders tested in Annex C a mean value of 31MPa is
found for the nine cylinders, which conﬁrms that it is possible to reach the desired strengths. Additionally,
the standard deviation of 1.9 indicates that it is possible to make concrete mixes with uniform properties.
Note that the compressive strength is higher that the mean strength found for the cylinders in Annex B due
to the duration in curing being larger for the cylinders in Annex C.
When comparing the different concrete mixes F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100, the behaviour
of F-RCA50 is in better compliance with the reference concrete F-NA40 than the remaining two mixes F-
RCA80 and F-RCA100. This suggests that the replacement ratio of the ﬁne fraction has a crucial effect on
the properties of both the fresh and the hardened concrete. The observed challenges of using substantial
replacement ratios of F-RCA may be related to the high water absorption levels of F-RCA, higher porosity
of F-RCA, or the large amount of ﬁne particles observed in F-RCA, which consists of residual cement paste
as well as small crushed particles.
Furthermore, the water absorption for the ﬁne fraction is determined using the methodology of determining
water absorption for sand, as no standard currently exists for determining the absorption of F-RCA. Due
41
==PAGE:58==
to the difference in the shape of particles and porosity of the ﬁne NA and F-RCA it is to be expected that
the method used for determining the water absorption of F-RCA is not necessarily sufﬁcient. Therefore, it
is likely that the value used for the water absorption of the F-RCA fraction may be severely overestimated
in the mix designs, thus resulting in a higher water to cement ratio and consequently a lower compressive
strength.
In general it is to be taken into consideration that the experiments carried out with regard to the ﬁne fraction
of RCA are all in coherence with the current standards for sand, which is anticipated to cause deviations.
This tendency underlines the necessity of reevaluating and updating the standards that deﬁne how material
properties are to be determined, as knowing the properties of the F-RCA allows for a higher degree of
homogeneity in the concrete mixes. For the coarse fraction of RCA the coherence between NA and RCA
is signiﬁcantly better than for the ﬁne fraction, and the deviations in material properties are thought to be
minor in most cases. Alternatively, an iterative approach is beneﬁcial to employ when designing concrete
mixes as a lot can be learned from a trial and error process.
Evaluating the performance of the aggregates used in the experiments it is found that it is sensible to
continue the experimental work regarding the casting of beams with the concrete mix F-RCA50 as this is
considered a good and reliable alternative to traditional concrete. The behaviour of the remaining concrete
mixes casted with RCA is more questionable, and this underlines the advantages of carrying out several
iterations during the process of designing each concrete mix, which has unfortunately not been possible
due to the time schedule of the current project. In general the observations made for the different concrete
mixes are to be considered having in mind that the ﬁndings are applicable for RCA of a certain strength
and quality, seeing that the RCA material used in the present project is observed to contain little to no
contaminants and known to have a strength of 50-55MPa [10].
A challenge that poses with regard to the building industry is the current building regulations and standards
where maximum amounts of replaced aggregates are subscribed in a manner that is very conservative, when
the results of the current project are considered. This obstacle is not easily dealt with as special permissions
have to be granted from public instances in order to legally replace larger amounts of aggregates than the
standards allow [12], which can be a lengthy process and requires a lot of documentation. In general the
requirements of using RCA are extensive, and from the current standard it is given which properties of
the RCA that are to be documented upon replacing different ratios of NA with RCA, which acknowledges
the importance of characterizing the donor concrete in the creation of new mix designs with RCA. Conse-
quently, the results of the cylinder tests imply that it is realistic to increase the allowed amount in the current
standards of both fractions of RCA in new concrete. However, this only applies for concrete used in the
passive environmental class, but the potential beneﬁts of using RCA remain large seeing that concrete in
42
==PAGE:59==
the passive environmental class constitutes approximately 60% of all concrete casted in Denmark [14].
As the theoretical calculations are based on lower bound methods the excess capacity of the beams is in
alignment with plasticity theory, which gives rise to debate whether the effectiveness factors commonly
used for structural concrete need to be adapted when verifying structures casted with recycled concrete. As
the recycled concrete exhibits a more plastic material behaviour it is sensible to increase the effectiveness
factors in order to increase the utilization of the recycled concrete, which is corroborated by the results of
the beam experiments. Upon disregarding the effectiveness factor in bending and increasing the expression
of the shear effectiveness factor from ⌫v = 0.7 −fc/200 for traditional concrete to ⌫v = 0.8 −fc/200 for
the concrete casted with RCA the theoretical load carrying capacities of the beams are closer to the actual
load carrying capacities, and the deviations between theory and experiments are slightly decreased. Adding
the increased effectiveness factor to the recycled concrete mixes and the usual effectiveness factor to the
reference concrete the plastic compressive strengths of the concrete mixes are apparent from Figure 7.1,
where it is observed that the recycled concrete F-RCA50 obtains larger capacity than the reference concrete
F-NA40. Thus, it is shown that recycled concrete can potentially be utilized better than traditional concrete.
Figure 7.1: Effective compressive strengths of the concrete mixes
Additionally, it is observed that the results of the experiments indicate that the calculation methods are con-
servative, seeing that all beams have a capacity that is between 10-20% higher than theoretical calculations
suggest. Overall, this is a good tendency seeing that it provides a margin in the safety of constructions,
however, it is debatable whether the margin of security is in fact too large when considering material con-
sumption. Given new requirements that aim to encourage the green transition in the building industry is it
highly relevant to take sustainability into account upon designing structures; especially concrete structures
as these account for the largest share of the environmental footprint in the building industry.
43
==PAGE:60==
Furthermore, as the tradition and use of concrete elements in Denmark are signiﬁcant, it is relevant to
consider the possibilities of using RCA in a wider perspective than on site casting, which has been the
perspective of the current report. The total production of concrete in Denmark is distributed into three
main categories being infrastructure, small scale buildings, and large scale buildings respectively, of which
infrastructure constitutes 37% and the remaining 63% is allocated into 16% for small scale buildings and
47% for large scale buildings. Annually, 29% of the concrete production for buildings in Denmark [18]
constitutes concrete elements, which is apparent from Figure 7.2. As concrete elements are being produced
in an orderly fashion using RCA as a partial replacement of NA ought to be fairly easily implemented in the
element industry provided that a security of supply is present. Additionally, one could argue that it does not
make a signiﬁcant difference whether transportation costs and emissions to the production plant are used
on the delivery of NA in comparison to RCA, to the extent that the transportation distances are similar.
Figure 7.2: Concrete production in Denmark, 2018 [18]
Currently, in the Danish building industry a lot of the construction demolition waste, CDW, produced dur-
ing demolition and renovation of structures is reused, and more than 90% of the concrete waste is crushed
and used as road ﬁll for infrastructure purposes [7]. The LCA study carried out to evaluate the potential of
decreasing the environmental footprint of structural concrete illustrates a decrease of 2% when considering
the GWP of F-NA40 in comparison with F-RCA50. As this decrease is not signiﬁcant it gives rise to con-
sider when it is sensible to use RCA as a replacement for NA in structural concrete compared to using RCA
as road ﬁll. The ﬁrst option results in a recycling of the concrete, as the RCA has a function corresponding
to NA in a new concrete, which facilitates a circular use of the materials. On the contrary, crushed concrete
used as road ﬁll is downcycled seeing that it is no longer in a circular material ﬂow when used as road ﬁll
and thereby loses value. However, the downcycling is still a signiﬁcantly better choice than depositing the
CDW at landﬁlls, where it will cause pollution.
The above considerations results in certain requirements for the purpose of using RCA as new aggregates
or road ﬁll respectively. CDW that has high traceability, where the strength and material properties can be
44
==PAGE:61==
properly documented, is ideal for structural purposes, whereas CDW that is less traceable, slightly contam-
inated, or has low strength can be used as road ﬁll due to the requirements and restrictions of the crushed
concrete for road ﬁll being less severe than for RCA. Both purposes of concrete CDW results in avoided
extraction of raw materials, and in order to utilize the CDW to the best extent possible it is necessary to
evaluate the possibilities related to transportation, economy, and environmental impact in context of what
is sensible for each speciﬁc case.
From a sustainability point of view it is important take factors such as logistics and security of supply
into consideration when determining the purpose of CDW, seeing that the environmental and economic
advantages of using RCA are largely dependant on transportation distances and costs. This underlines that
the use of concrete CDW as RCA is only sensible when the donor concrete can be locally sourced, due to
the environmental beneﬁts of RCA becoming absent when transport emissions increase. Similarly, large
transportation distances contradict using RCA in an economic perspective. Therefore, an advantageous
use of CDW as RCA is found when a construction site is coinciding with a demolition site as seen in the
demonstration case on Prags Boulevard in Amager. Here, the sorting and crushing of concrete was carried
out on site using a mobile recycling plant, after which new concrete was casted on site, thus practically
eliminating transportation emissions and costs.
Thus, all factors discussed above are to be taken into consideration upon designing structures with recycled
concrete, and that it is not necessarily sufﬁcient to simply design structures based on the material properties
and structural capacity of concrete.
45
==PAGE:62==
8
|
Conclusion
The current project seeks to evaluate the possibilities of replacing natural aggregates, NA, with recycled
concrete aggregates, RCA, with regard to the material properties of concrete. The characterization of ag-
gregates and cylinder testing conﬁrmed that RCA as a material is in compliance with the requirements
found in the current standards and thereby a suitable alternative for NA. Furthermore, it is conﬁrmed that
it is possible to obtain desirable compressive strengths when replacing NA with RCA, where the most ap-
plicable replacement ratio was found for concrete mix F-RCA50, where 100% of the coarse fraction and
50% of the ﬁne fraction is replaced with RCA. The ﬁndings indicate that the allowed replacement ratios
in the current standard can be increased with regard to the coarse fraction, whereas more caution is to be
taken upon replacing large ratios of the ﬁne fraction due to difﬁculties assessing parameters such as water
absorption and the inﬂuence of ﬁne particles.
The experimental work regarding the casting and testing of beams has led to a ﬁnding that the structural
behaviour of the beams is similar regardless of test conﬁguration and whether the beams are casted with
F-NA40 or F-RCA50. In all cases it is observed that the failure of the beams is caused by failure of the
reinforcement, which occurs after the initial cracking of the concrete has taken place in coherence with
the theory studied in the present project. In conclusion the yield condition derived for beams casted with
RCA exposed to combined impacts is conservative and thereby useful in the veriﬁcation of beams. The
mentioned ﬁndings all imply that recycled concrete is a reliable alternative to traditional concrete, seeing
that a material with predictable performance is necessary for employment in the industry.
Furthermore, the current project aims to assess the potential of decreasing the environmental footprint of
concrete by replacing NA with RCA. Overall, the results of the experimental work carried out indicate
that replacing NA with RCA in concrete is a feasible and sensible solution to considerations regarding the
handling of construction and demolition waste, CDW. Additionally, it is concluded that the use of RCA has
a minor impact on the global warming potential, GWP, of concrete, seeing that the GWP is reduced by 2%.
However, as the concept of sustainability is wider than only assessing the carbon footprint of concrete, a
signiﬁcant factor to consider is that an increased use of RCA will decrease resource scarcity, which will be
eventually an inevitable issue. In order to design sustainable buildings for future generations it is therefore
of grave importance to widen the perspective on how RCA can be utilized with regard to purpose, security
of supply, economy, and transportation amongst other aspects.
46
==PAGE:63==
9
|
Further Work
The aim of this project was to investigate the yield condition of beams casted with ﬁne recycled concrete
aggregates, F-RCA, and coarse recycled concrete aggregates, C-RCA, for beams exposed to bending, shear,
and torsion. Below, some of the subjects found relevant for further investigations are mentioned in relation
to the current thesis and its delimitations.
Seeing that it is very likely that the water absorption of the ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA, is
overestimated it is interesting to examine whether this can be more accurately determined using a different
method than provided in the current project. Similarly, as the time frame of the project did not allow for
several iterations to be carried out with regard to the design of the different concrete mixes, it is highly
relevant to investigate whether adjusting the mix designs during casting may provide a better recycled
concrete. Furthermore, it is of interest to investigate why concrete mix design F-RCA80 was found to have
a lower compressive strength than F-RCA100, seeing that the replacement ratio was larger for F-RCA100.
If the use of RCA is to be increased, there is a need of more in-depth knowledge on the causes of strength
reduction of recycled concrete.
As the test facilities provided for the beams at DTU were not sufﬁcient to fully investigate the yield con-
dition it is desirable to carry out further beam experiments, of which it would be compelling to vary the
reinforcement layout of the beams as well as the test set-up to induce shear failure or even torsional fail-
ure. Additionally, a further detailed experimental program would be beneﬁcial upon potentially revising the
effectiveness factors for concrete casted with RCA.
In relation to the Life Cycle Assessment, LCA, a more elaborate assessment containing all aspects of
an LCA is of importance for further work to obtain comprehensive knowledge about the environmental
impacts. A full LCA study could show the environmental impacts related to non renewable resources,
seeing that a consequential LCA will take the avoided extraction of raw materials in to consideration by
crediting these materials to other product systems.
Further work with the delimitations established in the current thesis such as structural behaviour during
serviceability, crack propagation, creep and shrinkage are relevant to consider. Research on the possibilities
of using ﬂy ash, microsilica and plasticizer in concrete mixes with RCA may also prove beneﬁcial for
47
==PAGE:64==
the future development of recycled concrete. Finally, further work can be carried out with regard to high
strength concrete and the remaining environmental classes in order to determine the applicability of recycled
concrete.

Mean Value and Standard Deviation
As each laboratory test is conducted three times under the same conditions, the mean value and standard
deviation are calculated to avoid showing excessive amounts of data in the main report. The mean value
is an average of the observations, while the standard deviation is the amount of dispersion of the values
observed. Thus, a low standard deviation suggests that the observed values tend towards the mean value,
whereas a higher standard deviation suggests that values are spread.
1.1
Mean Value
The mean value is determined by
µ = 1
n ·
n
X
i=1
xi
(1.1)
where
µ
is the mean value
n
is the number of observations
xi
is the value of each observation
1.2
Standard Deviation
The standard deviation is determined as the square root of the variance, which is the difference between
each observed value and the mean value. Thus, the variance is determined by
V ar = 1
n ·
n
X
i=1
(xi −µ)2
(1.2)
where
V ar
is the variance
n
is the number of observations
xi
is the value of each observation
µ
is the mean value of the observations
1
==PAGE:145==
And ﬁnally, the standard deviation is determined by
SD =
p
V ar
(1.3)
where
SD
is the standard deviation
V ar
is the variance

Introduction
This annex is compiled to determine the physical properties of the recycled concrete aggregates, RCA,
which are used to replace natural aggregates, NA, in the project Combined Impacts for Beams casted with
Fine and Coarse Recycled Concrete Aggregates. This is beneﬁcial when creating different concrete mix de-
signs, of which one will be chosen to cast the beams that are the basis of the project. In order to characterize
the recycled concrete aggregates, RCA, and develop different concrete mixes, a number of laboratory tests
are carried out. The current annex will describe the methods used to conduct each test as speciﬁed in the
respective European Standards as well as calculations performed in regard to determining the properties of
the aggregates. Table 1.1 summarizes which tests are carried out and their corresponding European Stan-
dards. For each test carried out the purpose, experimental procedure, results, data processing, and potential
sources of error are described, and ﬁnally a conclusion summarizes the overall ﬁndings.
Table 1.1: Overview of experiments
Laboratory test
European Standard
Particle size distribution
DS/EN 933-1
Water absorption and density
DS/EN 1097-6
Water content
DS/EN 1097-5
Cement content
-
Workability
DS/EN 12350-2
Air Content
DS/EN 12350-7
Compressive strength
DS/EN 12390-1:4
The recycled concrete aggregates used in the current annex originate from a demolished HOFOR-chimney
at Amager and are donated by Norrecco A/S. A detailed report [1] on the demolition of the chimney de-
scribes how the donor concrete is assessed to be suitable for recycling according to DS/EN 12620 [9]. The
donor concrete is found to have a compressive strength of 50-55MPa.
Usually, natural aggregates used in concrete mixes have diameters between 0-32mm and are divided into
two main fractions, namely ﬁne and coarse aggregates, where the coarse aggregates are further subdivided
into three fractions dependent on the diameter of the grain. In the current report the recycled concrete
aggregates are only divided into the main fractions. The fraction of ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates, F-
RCA, which corresponds to grain diameters between 0-4mm, and the fraction of coarse recycled concrete
1
==PAGE:160==
aggregates, C-RCA, that corresponds to gravel diameters between 4-22mm with no further subdivision.
The natural aggregates are divided into standardized fractions of seasand 0-4mm, and seagravel 4-8mm and
8-16mm respectively.
2
==PAGE:161==
2
|
Particle Size Distribution
The particle size distribution of an aggregate is a geometrical property that deﬁnes how the grain sizes of
a speciﬁc aggregate varies. The aim of this test is to determine the grain sizes of the recycled concrete
aggregates and compare the distribution with the particle size distribution found for natural aggregates.
2.1
Experimental Procedure
The particle size distributions for the ﬁne and coarse fractions of the recycled concrete aggregates as well
as the particle size distributions for the seasand and seagravel fractions are determined according to DS/EN
933-1 [10]. The experimental procedure consists of collecting a number of sieves with decreasing mesh
sizes on top of each other, which will divide the test portion of each aggregate into particle size classiﬁca-
tions according to the sieves used in the laboratory test. For the ﬁne fraction of RCA and the seasand the
relevant sieves have mesh sizes of 0.125mm, 0.25mm, 0.5mm, 1mm, 2mm, 4mm, and 8mm. Whereas for
the coarse fraction of RCA and the seagravel fractions, the relevant sieves have the mesh sizes of 2mm,
4mm, 8mm, 16mm, and 32mm. The chosen mesh sizes include the entire variation of the diameter of the
speciﬁc aggregate, and an additional sieve that is one size larger for the ﬁne fractions and one size smaller
for the coarse fractions, as it is relevant to ensure that the aggregate diameters are actually within the given
span. The sieves for the ﬁne and the coarse fractions respectively are shown in Figure 2.1.
(a) Sieves for the ﬁne fractions
(b) Sieves for the coarse fractions
Figure 2.1: Sieves for determination of particle size distribution
3
==PAGE:162==
As preparation for the test, portions of the aggregates are dried in the oven at 105 °C for 24 hours. Addi-
tionally, the mass of each test portion is determined according to DS/EN 933-1 [10], which is dependent
on the largest diameter of the considered aggregate. The minimum masses for the ﬁne fractions are 0.2kg
and the minimum masses for the coarse fractions vary between 0.6-10.0kg. The minimum masses for each
aggregate type according to DS/EN 933-1 along with the actual masses used in the test are summarized in
Table 2.1.
Table 2.1: Test portions for determination of particle size distribution

Upon placing a test portion in the sieve column, the sieves are attached to a mechanical vibrator and vibrated
for three minutes. After the sieving each sieve with retained material is weighed. For each aggregate type
the sieving test is carried out three times, to ensure the representativeness of the results.

found in Appendix A. The table shows the different sieve sizes, the mass of sieves and samples, and the
retained material, which determine the passing fraction that is given in kilograms and in percent.

From Table 2.3 it is apparent that Test 1 for the F-RCA fraction and Test 3 for the C-RCA fraction exceed
the limit of 1%. Thus, these tests are not qualiﬁed for further data processing and are therefore removed.
The ﬁne and coarse fractions for the NA and the RCA respectively are combined to get a complete particle
size distribution for each aggregate. The mean particle size distributions for both the NA and the RCA are
illustrated in Figure 2.2 alongside the upper and lower limits of the recommended particle size distribution,
which are determined according to the general grading requirements given in DS/EN 12620 [9]. However,
the requirements are given for the ﬁne and coarse fraction solely. This is accommodated by using the given
values for each fraction and interpolating for the intermediate values, which has led to the upper and lower
limits marked with the black dashed lines in Figure 2.2.
Figure 2.2: Particle size distribution for natural and recycled concrete aggregates along with the upper and lower limits
5
==PAGE:164==
From Figure 2.2 it is apparent that the distribution of the recycled concrete aggregates lies below the dis-
tribution of the natural aggregates, and corresponds fairly well with the general grading requirements. Ad-
ditionally, it can be observed for both the natural aggregates and the recycled concrete aggregates that the
majority of the particle sizes are distributed between 4-16mm. This is visible from the inclined slope which
is large in the speciﬁc area, meaning that a higher percentage of material is retained.
2.4
Errors
For the determination of the particle size distribution errors related to weighing of the material and the
sieves are to be expected. Such errors can be caused by using an inaccurate scale, and in order to decrease
the risk of inaccuracies due to weighing the same scale has been used for weighing all of the sieves for each
aggregate. Furthermore, throughout the sieving, some aggregates are stuck in the mesh of the sieves, which
will cause very slight weight differences of the sieves seeing that these are not re-weighed before the next
sieving.
2.5
Discussion
The sieving test shows that the particle size distribution of the RCA is closer to the required values com-
pared to the NA, which is not expected as the RCA are alternatives to the NA. It is difﬁcult to determine
what causes the NA to deviate from the required values seeing that DTU’s supplier has not provided any
information on the natural aggregates.
2.6
Partial Conclusion
The sieving tests emphasise that recycled concrete aggregates have particle size distributions that are appli-
cable for use in concrete mixes.
6
==PAGE:165==
3
|
Density and Water Absorption
The density and the water absorption of the aggregates are essential factors that inﬂuence the concrete mixes
considerably. The density used in concrete mix designs is the density of the aggregates in the saturated
surface dry state, SSD-state, since the SSD-state corresponds to the state of the aggregates in the fresh
concrete mixture. When the aggregates are in SSD-state the pores are water ﬁlled but the surface is dry, and
therefore no water will be absorbed or added to the concrete mix from the aggregates. Thus, the density in
SSD-state is used as a reference when adjusting the mix design with regard to the state of the aggregates
at the day of the casting. The aim of the current laboratory test is to determine the density and the water
absorption of the recycled concrete aggregates. The results are used to adjust the concrete mixes to prevent
that the aggregates will absorb free water and thereby affect the water to cement ratio of the mix design.
3.1
Experimental Procedure
The test is carried out for both the ﬁne and the coarse recycled aggregates in accordance with DS/EN 1097-6
[4] using the pyknometer method. As preparation for the determination of density and water absorption both
the ﬁne and coarse aggregates are dried in an oven at 105 °C for 24 hours until reaching a constant mass.
Three test portions of approximately 300g of the ﬁne fraction are investigated using 500ml pyknometers,
and three test portions of 800g of the coarse fraction are investigated using 1000ml pyknometers. According
to the standard the test portions of the coarse aggregates ought to be minimum 3.2kg, which is found by
interpolation of the values in Table 2, DS/EN 1097-6 [4]. However, using the test facilities at DTU it is not
possible to handle these large test portions due to the size of the pyknometers available.
The pyknometer method consists of immersing a test portion of the aggregate into demineralised water
ﬁlling the pyknometer approximately 75%, after which the pyknometer is placed in a desiccator, where a
vacuum is induced for roughly 24 hours. Upon removing the pyknometer from the desiccator, it is overﬁlled
with demineralised and air free water, and the cover is placed on top with no air trapped in the vessel after
which the pyknometer is dried and weighed. The further procedure of the test is dependent on whether ﬁne
or coarse aggregates are examined.
7
==PAGE:166==
For the ﬁne fraction the next step is to empty the pyknometer and collect the test portion in a beaker, and dry
the content to SSD-state using a pan, after which the test portion is weighed. The SSD-state is determined
in compliance with Annex F in DS/EN 1097-6 [4], where the ﬁne aggregates are placed in a cone and
compacted lightly 25 times before removing the cone and observing an almost complete collapse with a
visible peak and angular slopes, which is apparent from Figure 3.1c.
(a) F-RCA in pyknometers
(b) F-RCA placed in cone
(c) F-RCA in achieved SSD-state
Figure 3.1: Determination of density and water absorption for F-RCA
For the coarse fraction the SSD-mass of the test portions are determined by drying the aggregates with
kitchen towels until the surface is seemingly dry, after which the test portion is weighed. The pyknometers
and the coarse fractions in wet and SSD-state are shown in Figure 3.2.
(a) C-RCA in pyknometers
(b) C-RCA wet
(c) C-RCA in achieved SSD-state
Figure 3.2: Determination of density and water absorption for C-RCA
8
==PAGE:167==
When all measurements are conducted the density and the water absorption of the aggregates are deter-
mined. The saturated surface dry particle density is determined by
⇢SSD = ⇢w ·

M1

M2
is the mass of the pyknometer containing the saturated surface dry test portion and water
M3
is the mass of the pyknometer ﬁlled with water only
Likewise, the water absorption is determined by
WA24 = 100 · (M1 −M4)

where

RCA fractions are shown in Table 3.1 for Test 1. The remaining results are shown in Appendix B, Table

Data Processing
The densities and the water absorption values are determined for each fraction, and the results are found in

Based on the found results the mean densities and water absorption are determined, these are given in Table
3.4 and 3.5 along with the standard deviation. The standard deviation is an indication of how scattered the
observation is compared to the mean value, an elaborate explanation is found in Annex A.

3

3.4
Errors
The rather large variation between the test portion according to the standard and the actual mass used for
the C-RCA fraction could be an error that inﬂuences the results. The SSD-state is determined by means
10
==PAGE:169==
of visual evaluation for both the ﬁne and the coarse fractions, which makes it difﬁcult to ensure that the
observed state is the actual saturated surface dry state. When determining the SSD-state for the F-RCA
fraction, the shape of the cone is compared to what sand should look like in SSD-state, which could give
incorrect results due to difference between the shape of the F-RCA particles and sand. During the test it
is observed that ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates have a different way of packing compared to the ﬁne
natural aggregates. This could inﬂuence the found densities and water absorption values. Another error is
the possibility of using an imprecise scale. The uncertainty is sought minimized by using the same scale
throughout the test.
3.5
Discussion
The density and the water absorption for the coarse fraction, C-RCA, are given by Norrecco [1] and the
values are compared to the values found by the laboratory tests. The density is given as 2480kg/m3 and the
given water absorption percentage is 3.6%. The found mean values are 2540kg/m3 and 3% respectively,
and the variations are considered as a minor difference that could arise from the errors in the performance
of the laboratory tests. When considering the standard deviations found for the densities, the values are
14.5 and 24.4 for the F-RCA and C-RCA respectively. These are considered rather high, but are at the
same time expected due to the methods being solely based on the visual evaluation when determining the
SSD-state. The same tendency is seen for the water absorption where the standard deviation is 6.7 and 1.2
for the F-RCA and C-RCA respectively.
3.6
Partial Conclusion
The values found for the F-RCA are used in the concrete mix designs, and for the C-RCA the density and
water absorption values given by Norrecco are used. For NA the standardised values of density and water
absorption are used as this is common practice in the industry.
11
==PAGE:170==
4
|
Water Content
The water content of the aggregates inﬂuences the amount of water needed in the concrete mixes, therefore
the aim of this test is to determine the water content in the different aggregates. The water content is
determined for both the recycled concrete aggregates and the natural aggregates, which is used to adjust the
concrete mix designs. To get valid results three test portions from each fraction are investigated. The tests
are executed on the day of the casting to get the most accurate results.
4.1
Experimental Procedure
The mass of the test portions are determined according to DS/EN 1097-5 [3]. The minimum mass for the
ﬁne fractions is 0.2kg which is used for both the ﬁne natural aggregates and the ﬁne recycled aggregates.
The mass of coarse aggregates is dependent on the largest diameter of the aggregate considered, which
varies for the different coarse fractions. The required and the actual masses for each fraction are shown in
Table 4.1, where smaller test portions are used for the coarse aggregates due to capacity limitations.
Table 4.1: Overview of required and actual masses
Fraction
Required Mass [kg]
Actual Mass [kg]
Seasand, 0-4mm
0.2
0.2
Seagravel, 4-8mm
1.6
0.5
Seagravel, 8-16mm
3.2
1.0
Fine Fraction, F-RCA
0.2
0.5
Coarse Fraction, C-RCA
4.4
1.0
Each test portion is weighed in the received state and then dried in a microwave oven for three minutes.
The test portion is then cooled to ambient air temperature and weighed again. Next, the test portion is dried
in the microwave oven for additional three minutes, cooled and weighed again. This is repeated until the
deviation between the masses is less than 0.1% which ensures constant mass. The water content of the
aggregates is determined by
w = M1 −M3
M3
· 100
(4.1)

where
w
is the water content of the test portion
M1
is the mass of the test portion
M3
is the constant mass of dried test portion

results are shown in Appendix C in Tables B.C.1, B.C.2, B.C.3, B.C.4, and B.C.5.

and B.C.5. From Table 4.3 it is seen that the deviation between the weighed data is less than 0.1% which

4.4
Errors
With regard to the water content a possible error is related to the uncertainties of the scale used. Another
parameter that may potentially inﬂuence the results is the use of smaller test portions than the masses
prescribed in the relevant standard DS/EN 1097-5 [3], as using the prescribed masses was not possible due
to the equipment available at DTU. Another error could be the use of a microwave oven instead of the
ventilated oven as prescribed in the standard, however this is considered to have a minor inﬂuence due to
the deviations between the masses being less than 0.1%.
4.5
Discussion
When comparing the mean values of the ﬁne fractions it is seen that the water content of the F-RCA is
higher than the value found for the seasand. This could be related to the high water absorption found for the
F-RCA in Section 3.3 Table 3.5, whereas the water absorption for seasand is substantially lower generally
around 4% [11]. Furthermore, the mean water content of seagravel fraction 8-16mm is higher than expected,
which can be due to the smaller test portion used. Also, the standard deviation for this fraction is higher
than for the remaining fractions, which is an indication of larger differences between the observation and
the mean value.
4.6
Partial Conclusion
The water content of each aggregate type is used to adjust the overall water content of the concrete mix
designs in order to secure a constant water to cement ratio. In conclusion it is observed that the water
content is larger for the RCA than the NA in the ﬁne fraction, whereas it is smaller for the RCA than the
NA in the coarse fraction of 8-16mm.
14
==PAGE:173==
5
|
Cement Content
In contrast to natural aggregates leftover cement will be attached to the crushed concrete aggregates, and
it is not possible to determine whether this will be active or not in the new concrete mix. However, it is
relevant to determine the amount of leftover cement as this may potentially contribute to the strength of the
new concrete.
5.1
Experimental Procedure
The cement content is determined for the F-RCA fraction, and the test is carried out according to a procedure
given by ZeroWaste Gruppen, since no standard is found on this speciﬁc laboratory test. The experimental
procedure can be found in Appendix D. A portion of the F-RCA fraction is crushed into powder using a
vibratory disc mill for 10 seconds. Three test portions of approximately 50g along with ﬁlters and trays
are weighed on a two decimal scale. Each test portion is placed in a container with 200ml demineralised
water of 48°C. The test container is placed in a fume hood, where 30ml of nitric acid is added to each test
portion. After a while 10ml nitric acid is reapplied to ensure that all of the cement is dissolved. Next, each
test portion is poured through a ﬁlter and thereby divided into wet powder and liquid, after which the ﬁlter
containing the wet powder and the tray are weighed. The ﬁlter and the powder portions are dried at 105°C
for 24 hours and weighed yet again. The cement content is determined by
C = M0 −M3

5.5
Discussion
The leftover cement can affect the water to cement ratio by absorbing water and thereby leave the concrete
more dry than anticipated, and therefore the rather high cement content of 30% can potentially affect the
mix designs. It is not possible to determine the amount of leftover cement that will react in the new concrete
mix, and ideally this could be taken into account.
16
==PAGE:175==
5.6
Partial Conclusion
The cement content is determined to obtain a better understanding of what recycled concrete aggregates are
comprised of, and which factors may inﬂuence a concrete mix. However, the cement content is not taken
into consideration in the concrete mixes, as this is considered as out of the scope of this project.

==PAGE:176==
6
|
Concrete Mix
When creating a mix design it is necessary to take factors such as which environment the concrete will be
exposed to and what strength requirement into consideration, as these factors greatly affect the properties
of the concrete mix. Upon developing a mix design the two main tasks are composing the cement paste
and composing the distribution of aggregates. For common concrete the cement paste will determine the
strength as this depends on the water to cement ratio. In order to ensure that the concrete gets the desired
compression strength and take uncertainties of concrete production into account, the mix design is devel-
oped for concrete that has an additional strength of 5-7MPa more than what is actually desired. Finally, the
distribution between ﬁne and coarse aggregates is chosen. In the current project it is prioritized to avoid
adding additives to the concrete such as ﬂy ash, microsilica, and plasticizer in order to focus only on the
inﬂuence of the aggregates. However, it is to be expected that the rather large amount of dust from the
crushing of the concrete behaves somewhat similar to ﬂy ash or microsilica as this affects the density of the
cement paste, which is not taken into account upon designing the concrete mixes.
6.1
Experimental Procedure
In the current project ﬁve different concrete mixes with varying contents of natural aggregates and recycled
concrete aggregates are investigated. The characterization of the aggregates form the basis of creating
mix designs in compliance with a desired compression strength of 20MPa in the passive environmental
class. The w/c-ratio of 0.71 is kept constant due to the strength requirement of 27MPa in the design mix.
Additionally, Table 6.1 and Figure 6.1 describe the distribution of both ﬁne and coarse aggregates as well as
the distribution of natural and recycled concrete aggregates in the concrete mixes named F-NA50, F-NA40,
F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 respectively.
Table 6.1: Distribution of coarse and ﬁne aggregates in the concrete mixes
Mix
Distribution of Aggregates
F-NA50
100% NA 4-16mm, 100% NA 0-4mm, aggregate ratio 50/50
F-NA40
100% NA 4-16mm, 100% NA 0-4mm, aggregate ratio 60/40
F-RCA50
100% C-RCA 4-22mm, 50% NA 0-4mm, 50% F-RCA 0-4mm
F-RCA80
100% C-RCA 4-22mm, 20% NA 0-4mm, 80% F-RCA 0-4mm
F-RCA100
100% C-RCA 4-22mm, 100% F-RCA 0-4mm
18
==PAGE:177==
To accommodate a reasonable workability of the concrete the two reference mixes F-NA50 and F-NA40
have been used to determine the optimal distribution between ﬁne and coarse aggregates. Concrete mix
F-NA50 is designed in compliance with the distribution method given in Table 4 in [11], in which the
percentage mass of the ﬁne fraction is determined by linear interpolation to be 50%. The distribution leads
to a very dry concrete with a low slump that is considered not applicable for practical use. As a result of
this it is chosen to increase the percentage mass of the coarse fraction to 60% while decreasing the mass
of the ﬁne fraction to 40% in mix F-NA40, which provides signiﬁcantly better workability of the concrete,
and therefore this aggregate distribution is chosen for the remaining mix designs. This is graphically shown
in Figure 6.1 where the percentages are distributed from the total mass of the concrete batch. Thus, the
naming of each mix design is based on the replacement ratio of the ﬁne fraction of aggregates seeing that
the entire coarse fraction is replaced in the mix designs using recycled concrete aggregates as an alternative
to natural aggregates.
Figure 6.1: Overview of mix designs
The concrete is produced in a mixer where the aggregates are initially poured from largest to smallest
particle size, after which the material is mixed in a sequence of mixing the dry material three minutes, then
adding the water and mixing three minutes, pausing three minutes, and ﬁnally remixing the materials for one
minute. The mixing is carried out with this sequence for all ﬁve concrete batches to ensure a homogeneous
concrete mass and a uniform mixing procedure.
19
==PAGE:178==
6.2
Results
From the characterization of the recycled aggregates ﬁve different concrete mixes are developed aiming
for a compressive strength of minimum 20MPa. The concrete mixes have a constant water to cement ratio
of 0.71, and the variation between them is the distribution of natural and recycled aggregates as shown in
Figure 6.1. For concrete mix F-NA50 and F-NA40 35L of concrete is made to ensure a sufﬁcient amount
of concrete for six cylinders and an air content test. The ﬁnal mix designs for the reference concrete mixes
are shown in Table 6.2.

6.3
Errors
For the design of the different concrete mixes no test batches are made, and therefore the actual air content
of each concrete mix is not taken into account in the water to cement ratio. Additionally, challenges are
represented upon weighing of the material as it is difﬁcult to ensure the exact amounts and adding the
20
==PAGE:179==
material in the concrete mixer with no residual material left in the buckets. Furthermore, as the amounts
used for the mixing are not very large a source of error may occur as it is a challenge to make certain that
the aggregate used is fully representative for the particle size distribution of each aggregate.
6.4
Discussion
The sources of error mentioned above may potentially inﬂuence the strength of the concrete as not con-
sidering the actual air content will most likely cause a slight decrease of the compression strength of the
concrete mixes due to the actual air content being somewhat higher than 1%.
6.5
Partial Conclusion
Following the procedure described above the concrete mix designs for both the reference batches and the
batches using recycled concrete aggregates ought to provide a mean compressive strength of at least 20MPa.
From the main conclusion in Section 10 it is apparent whether the desired compressive strength is obtained.

==PAGE:180==
7
|
Workability
The workability of the concrete is relevant when considering the casting process, as the texture of the fresh
concrete determines how applicable the concrete is in practice. The workability of the concrete mixes
are tested by measuring the slump. The purpose of the slump test is to determine the consistency and
workability of the fresh concrete and investigate if the slump changes over time for the mixes with recycled
concrete aggregates.
7.1
Experimental Procedure
The slump tests are performed according to DS/EN 12350-2 [5], and are carried out immediately after the
mixing process for the different concrete mixes. The test is carried out once for the reference concrete
mixes, F-NA50 and F-NA40, while for the mixes consisting of recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA50, F-
RCA80, and F-RCA100, the slump test is carried out three times. The ﬁrst test is performed initially as for
the reference mixes, after which the test is carried out again after 15 minutes and 30 minutes respectively.
This is done to investigate how the slump changes over time for mixes with recycled concrete aggregates.
Note, that the concrete is not remixed in between the slump tests. The apparatuses used for the tests are a
metal cone, a table with clamps, a compacting rod and a ruler. In Figure 7.1 the test procedure is shown.
Figure 7.1: Slump test procedure
As preparation the metal cone is clamped to the table after which the fresh concrete is poured into the
metal cone. The cone is ﬁlled approximately one third of the cone height and then compacted by uniformly
distributing 25 strokes across the concrete with the compacting rod. The compacting rod is lifted a bit from
22
==PAGE:181==
the table to ensure not hitting the bottom when compacting the concrete, as this will cause an additional
unwanted vibration. The next one-third is poured and compacted by 25 strokes, the rod is held a bit over
the ﬁrst layer and thereby only compacting the second layer. Finally, the third layer is ﬁlled and compacted,
and the cone is reﬁlled if necessary. Afterwards the surface is levelled, and the metal cone is pulled straight
upwards in one move. The reversed metal cone is used to place the steel rod and then measure the distance
between the top of the cone and the concrete. The slump is measured from the centre of the concrete cone
to the bottom of the steel rod, where the slump is reported rounding up to the next 10mm.
7.2
Results
The slump is found as the inital slump for the two reference concrete mixes F-NA50 and F-NA40. For
the mixes consisting of recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100, the slump is
measured three times. The measured slumps for each concrete mix are shown in Table 7.1.
Table 7.1: Slump of the different concrete mixes
Mix
Slump, initial [mm]
Slump, 15 min [mm]
Slump, 30 min [mm]
F-NA50
30
-
-
F-NA40
70
-
-
F-RCA50

F-RCA80

Figure 7.2 shows the slumps for the concrete mixes consisting of natural aggregates, F-NA50 and F-NA40,
where it is seen that mix F-NA50 has a more solid and compact form compared to mix F-NA40. For the
remaining slumps of the different concrete mixes see Appendix F, Figures B.F.1, B.F.2, B.F.3, and B.F.4.
(a) Initial slump mix F-NA50
(b) Initial slump mix F-NA40
Figure 7.2: Initial slumps for the concrete mixes Consisting of NA for mixes F-NA50 and F-NA40
23
==PAGE:182==
7.3
Errors
An error that could affect the results is the determination of the three layers which are visually evaluated
and thereby hard to execute identically each time. This could inﬂuence the compaction and thereby the
measurements. Another error that could occur is under- or over-compacting which occurs due to the position
of the compacting rod, which is visually evaluated and could therefore lead to layers being vibrated twice.
Additionally, a source of error could appear when removing the metal cone, as this shall be done in one
move and without any twisting or turning of the metal cone. This is hard to ensure since the concrete can
be sticky and thereby hard to get to let go of the metal cone, and seeing that residue of old dry concrete
inside the cone from earlier slump tests creates some friction upon lifting the cone. The errors are sought
minimized by letting the same person execute the same procedures for each test.
7.4
Discussion
Traditional concrete is delivered with slumps between 60mm and 200mm [2]. The slump of 30mm found
for concrete mix F-NA50, which is the mix with an equal distribution between the ﬁne and the coarse
aggregate fractions, as depicted in Figure 6.1, is rather low compared to the range for traditional concrete.
This observation lead to the creation of reference mix F-NA40, where the relation between the ﬁne and
the coarse fraction are 40% to 60%, which is maintained for the RCA mixes F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and
F-RCA100.
The following mixes; F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 are thus within the traditional slump
range when considering the initial slumps and the slumps after 15 minutes. However, concrete mix F-
RCA40 develops a slump after 30 minutes which is lower than 60mm, and this may cause workability
issues seeing that concretes with small slumps are harder to work with and will need more vibrating. When
comparing the initial slumps of the mixes consisting of recycled concrete aggregates, concrete mix F-
RCA80 is deviating substantially. Concrete mix F-RCA80 has the highest slump and is not following the
tendency found for mix F-RCA40 and F-RCA100, where the slump seems to increase with the increase of
replaced natural aggregates. This could be an indication of some sort of error in the mix.
7.5
Partial Conclusion
Overall, the results show that the mixes F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 are applicable for
casting according to the traditional slump ranges.
24
==PAGE:183==
8
|
Air Content
The air content inﬂuences the water to cement ratio and is therefore relevant to take into consideration when
working with concrete mixes. The aim of the test is to determine the air content of the different concrete
mixes and thereby evaluate whether the air content is overestimated in the mix designs.
8.1
Experimental Procedure
The test is carried out according to DS/EN 12350-7 [6], and executed for each concrete mix by pouring the
freshly made concrete into a pressure meter. The pressure meter is ﬁlled one third at the time and vibrated
in between the layers with a vibrating platform until no air bubbles are visible. Afterwards, the lid is placed
and water is poured into one side of the pressure meter until it comes out on the other side, which is done to
have as little entrapped air in the pressure meter as possible. Next, air is pumped into the the pressure meter
until the arrow is below zero, which is then adjusted to zero. Finally, pressure is applied by pressing the
green button, and when the arrow stops moving the current air content is determined. The pressure meter is
shown in Figure 8.1.
Figure 8.1: Determination of air content with pressure meter
8.2
Results
The measured air content for each concrete mix is shown in Table 8.1.
25
==PAGE:184==
Table 8.1: Air content of the different concrete mixes
Mix
Air Content [%]
F-NA50
1.3
F-NA40
1.6
F-RCA50
1.5
F-RCA80
1.1
F-RCA100
1.8
8.3
Errors
For the determination of the current air content in the different concrete mixes over-vibration is an example
of an error that may affect the results as over-vibration can cause a loss of entrapped air. Additionally, the
pressure meter needs to be recalibrated every once in a while as it is a sensitive tool. Thus, some small
deviations are to be expected if the apparatus is not properly calibrated.
8.4
Discussion
All of the measured values of the air content are found to be larger than the values used in the concrete mix
designs. Usually, the natural air content is somewhere between 1-2% [11], and the air content in all mix
designs is set to 1%. The concrete mixes F-NA50 and F-RCA80 are closest to the used air content value of
1% with measurements of 1.3% and 1.1% respectively. The air content for the mixes F-NA40, F-RCA50,
and F-RCA100 are 1.6%, 1.5%, and 1.8% respectively. Therefore, the larger air contents for the mixes F-
NA40, F-RCA50, and F-RCA100 will inﬂuence the w/c ratio and consequently affect the mix designs and
the strength of the concrete batches. Ideally, a test batch for each of the concrete mixes could be produced
in order to take the air content into account in the mix designs and thus result in potentially more accurate
mixes.
8.5
Partial Conclusion
In this project it is chosen not to spend time on adjusting the mix designs due to the values of the air content
all being within the usual range. Nevertheless, the potential inﬂuence of the different air contents is kept in
mind.
26
==PAGE:185==
9
|
Compressive Strength
The compressive strength is the most commonly used mechanical property of concrete, and factors that
affect the compressive strength are the water to cement ratio, the cement type, and the air content of the
concrete mix amongst others. Furthermore, the strength of the concrete is directly dependent on the age
of the concrete as strength develops over time. The strength is deﬁned as the uniaxial compressive impact
that crushes the concrete. The aim of the test is to determine and compare the compressive strengths of the
different concrete mixes F-NA50, F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100. The tests will provide
data to depict the stress-strain relationship for each of the cylinders in the different mixes, and the peak
value of the stress-strain relationship corresponds to the compressive strength of the concrete. Additionally,
the friction angles of the different mixes are evaluated after crushing of the concrete cylinders.
9.1
Experimental Procedure
Six cylinders are casted for each mix design F-NA50, F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 with
the following dimensions, a height of 200mm and a diameter of 100mm. The casting procedure is executed
in compliance with DS/EN 12390-2 [7], where the cylinders are casted by ﬁlling the mould one half,
followed by vibration and then ﬁlled with the remaining half and vibrated again. To achieve full compaction
a mechanical vibration platform is used as shown in Figure 9.1a. The cylinders are vibrated until no air
bubbles appear on the surface, and the objective of the vibration is to achieve the necessary compaction
without over-vibrating the concrete, as this can lead to a loss of entrapped air or separation.
(a) Cylinders on vibration platform
(b) Cylinders placed in water
Figure 9.1: Casting and curing of concrete cylinders
27
==PAGE:186==
Next, the concrete surface is levelled with the lid and thereafter clamped. The clamps are tightened and the
moulds are stored horizontally for 24 hours. The moulds are then removed and the cylinders are cured in
water at a constant temperature of 20°C in order to keep the concrete hydrated. In Figure 9.1 the casting
and curing of the cylinders are shown.
The cylinders are kept in water until the compression test is executed according to DS/EN 12390-3 [8]. The
compression tests are carried out after 28 days of curing for cylinder mix F-NA50, F-RCA50, F-RCA80,
and F-RCA100, whereas cylinder mix F-NA40 is tested after 29 days. The compression tests are carried
out using an Instron-8508 machine with a capacity of 5000kN, where force is applied to the specimens by
means of a hydraulic actuator. An extensometer is attached to the cylinders in order to measure the vertical
displacements during the test. The extensometer is connected to a data-logger that measures the load, the
change in length due to compression, and the electrical supply. Each cylinder surface is brieﬂy wiped off
before testing the cylinder with the attached extensometer is placed in the test machine, after which the test
is started and data-logging begins. Next, the upper platen is slowly positioned on top of the cylinder, and the
test machine is then started. The load is applied with a loading rate of 0.6 ± 0.2MPa/s, which corresponds
to a load of 4.71 kN/s. The load is applied until failure.
Figure 9.2: Compression test setup
To ensure a satisfactory procedure of the tests, the failure types are assessed and compared to failure types
given in DS/EN 12390-3 [8]. Figure 9.3 shows the satisfactory failure types of cylinder specimens.
28
==PAGE:187==
Figure 9.3: Satisfactory failure of cylinder specimen according to [8]

In the data processing of the stress-strain relation, the results of each compression test are stored by a data-
logger which receives signals from both the testing machine and the extensometer. In order to interpret the
results it is necessary to do a calibration of the equipment, which forms the basis of processing the data into
the desired units. The calibration is carried out manually by placing the extensometer on a steel rod and
continuously compressing the rod while noting the migration distance, Mdistance [mm], using a precision
surface gauge as well as noting the electrical supply, Esupply [V], and the signal from the extensometer,
Esignal [mV]. The calibration set-up is apparent in Figure 9.6.
Figure 9.6: Calibration set-up
The migration distances, electrical supplies, and the electrical signals are obtained for six data points with
0.5mm between them, after which a linear relation between displacement and the electric signal is estab-
lished resulting in the trend line shown in Figure 9.7.
31
==PAGE:190==
Figure 9.7: Calibration of measuring equipment
Knowing an expression for the trend line allows for the displacement to be calculated from the data stored
with the data-logger, and thus the strain can be determined according to Equation 9.3. Similarly, the force
is calculated from the signals received by the data logger from the test machine as this supplies 400kN/V.
Thus, it is also possible to calculate the stress according to Equation 9.2, and the stress-strain relation can
then be depicted. For each of the cylinders tested the compressive strength is determined, and the mean
compressive strength of each concrete mix is shown in Table 9.2. The stress-strain relation is established
based on the raw calibrated data, and in Figure 9.8 the relation is shown for the reference concrete mix,
F-NA40, whereas the remaining stress-strain relations are shown in Appendix G, Figure B.G.6.
Figure 9.8: Stress-strain relation based on calibrated raw data
32
==PAGE:191==
The stress-strain relation is increasing linearly in the beginning of the compression test until a more parabolic
shape is obtained at the peak, after which the data is scattered randomly. Due to the data-logging being
started before lowering the upper platen of the test machine and being stopped after crushing of the con-
crete, the data obtained in the beginning and end of each compression test is rather uneven, especially after
failure has occurred. In order to obtain a more accurate stress-strain relation the raw calibrated data is
sorted for both the data measurements in the beginning and the end of the data set, seeing that the scattered
measurements are removed and a new origin is added in order for all of the stress-strain relations to start
in (0,0). Based on the sorted data the upgraded stress-strain relations are plotted, and Figure 9.9 shows the
the plots for concrete mix F-NA40 and F-RCA50, whereas the remaining stress-strain relations are shown
in Appendix G, Figure B.G.7.
For concrete mix F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 a mean stress-strain relation based on mix design F-
NA40 is plotted in order to emphasize the difference between traditional concrete and concrete casted with
recycled concrete aggregates. The mean stress-strain relation based on concrete mix F-NA40 is depicted
by choosing 45 x-coordinates corresponding to the strain, after which the y-coordinates of each mix design
belonging to the chosen x-coordinates are determined using linear interpolation between the two data points
closest to each x-value. Next, the mean stress is calculated for the chosen x-coordinates, and in Figure 9.9b
the mean curve is marked with the black dotted line named F-RCA50.
(a) Stress-strain relation based sorted data for mix F-NA40
(b) Stress-strain relation based on sorted data for mix F-RCA50
Figure 9.9: Stress-strain relation based on sorted data
The peak of the stress-strain relation shows the maximum stress and thereby the compressive strength. The
compressive strengths for each cylinder test are shown in Appendix G Table B.G.1 whereas the mean values
for the concrete mixes along with the standard deviation are shown in table 9.2.

9.4
Errors
The results of the compressive tests are to a great extent determined by the mix design of each concrete
batch. However, there are certain potential sources of error related to the casting of the cylinders, namely
if the cylinders are not properly vibrated or the surfaces are not properly levelled. Moreover, as clamps are
used to tighten the lid on the cylinder molds, the cylinders are placed horizontally until they are removed
from the molds, which has created slightly unlevelled surfaces along the cylinder sides facing upwards.
These geometrical irregularities are likely to inﬂuence the results of the compressive tests.
Besides from the geometry of the cylinders the distribution of aggregates in each cylinder may also have a
potential impact on the results, as a satisfactory compaction of the aggregates is important upon ensuring the
mechanical properties of the concrete. The compaction is gained by vibrating the concrete while visually
assessing whether large air bubbles are ascending, and when no bubbles are visible the vibration is stopped.
Too little vibration will cause poor compaction, whereas too much vibration increases the risk of separation
between the cement paste and the aggregates and the risk of losing entrapped air. Thus, the compaction
has the potential to affect the results, and as the vibration assessment is visual it poses challenges to ensure
consistency.
9.5
Discussion
Geometrical irregularities are found on all cylinders to a certain extent, however in general the desired
dimensions are achieved within a range of ± 2mm. The surfaces of each cylinder inﬂuence the results
directly since the cross sectional area of the specimens is used to determine the compressive strength. A
number of cylinders are found to have some rough surfaces which could inﬂuence the results, these are
shown below in Figures 9.10 and 9.11 next to a specimen with an optimal surface.
34
==PAGE:193==
(a) Uneven surface F-NA40-4
(b) Even surface F-RCA50-1
Figure 9.10: Geometrical irregularities along sides of cylinder
(a) Uneven surface F-RCA50-4
(b) F-RCA100-3 with stud
(c) Even surface F-NA50-1
Figure 9.11: Geometrical Irregularities on top of cylinder
The friction angles show good correlation between the theoretical value of 37° and the determined values
for each mix design. For mix design F-NA50 and F-NA40 which consist of traditional concrete it is to be
expected that the friction angles are aligned with theory, and the concrete mixes F-RCA50 and F-RCA100
consisting of different amounts of recycled concrete aggregates show the same tendency with mean values
of 37.50° and 38.75° respectively. Cylinders of concrete mix F-RCA80 have the largest deviation when
compared to the theoretical value with a mean value of 35.00°, as well as the largest standard deviation.
The standard deviations are lowest for concrete mix F-NA50 and F-NA40 which indicates that these values
are rather close to the mean value. Additionally, the standard deviations are a bit higher for concrete mix
F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 which show a larger difference between the recorded values.
The stress-strain relations found for the concrete mixes consisting of traditional concrete F-NA50 and F-
NA40 ought to have a concrete strain of 2.0h at the maximum stress, which is generally achieved. For the
concrete mixes using recycled concrete aggregates some variations from theory are detected regarding the
cylinders F-RCA40-1, F-RCA40-2 and F-RCA40-3, where the strain at maximum stress is found between 2-
2.5h, and cylinder F-RCA50-4 is disregarded due to large deviations compared to the remaining cylinders.
35
==PAGE:194==
The same tendency is observed for the strains found for mix design F-RCA80 and F-RCA100.
The mean compressive strength for all concrete mixes, except for mix F-RCA80, are larger than the antici-
pated value of 20MPa. Concrete mix F-RCA80 reached the lowest compressive strength with a mean value
of 17.54MPa which is assumed to be caused by an unknown error during the casting procedure. The as-
sumption is supported by concrete mix F-RCA80 being the mix with the largest slump, which could indicate
that too much water was used in the batch. The assumption of mixture F-RCA80 being erroneous is further
supported by the mean values found for mix F-RCA50 and F-RCA100, where a tendency of decreasing
strength with increasing use of recycled concrete aggregates is detected.
9.6
Partial Conclusion
Based on the failure shapes the compressive tests are considered satisfying by showing the expected behav-
ior, which also applies to the found friction angles, compressive strength and stress-strain relations for the
different concrete mixes.
36
==PAGE:195==
10
|
Conclusion
The characterization of ﬁne and coarse recycled concrete aggregates formed the basis of creating mix de-
signs for the concrete batches F-NA50, F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 aiming for a com-
pressive strength of 20MPa. Determining the physical properties of the RCA used to replace the NA is
paramount in order to evaluate the potential of replacing larger amounts of the natural aggregates than what
is allowed according to the Danish regulations today. From the characterization it can be concluded that
majority of the physical properties of the RCA are in coherence with the physical properties of natural ag-
gregates, which is underlined by the particle size distribution and the density of the recycled aggregates in
particular. However, it is also concluded that the water absorption of the F-RCA is very high, and that the
test method may overestimate the actual water absorption as it is actually intended for sand and not RCA.
The mix designs are developed to achieve a compressive strength of 20MPa after 28 days of hardening, and
this is accomplished for the mixes F-NA50, F-NA40, F-RCA50, and F-RCA100, whereas mix F-RCA80
does not comply with the desired strength. In this regard a further investigation of mix design F-RCA80
is necessary to determine whether the failure to comply with the compressive strength requirements is
caused by human error or something entirely different. When disregarding mix design F-RCA80, it can be
concluded that it is possible to fully replace the natural aggregates and still achieve the desired compressive
strength. However it should be kept in mind, that the found values are mean values and not the 5% fractile
which is usually used when characterizing concrete. Nonetheless, it is also concluded that the compressive
strength decreases upon an increased content of recycled concrete aggregates. Furthermore, the different
concrete mix designs created using both RCA and NA all meet the traditional slump ranges used when
casting fresh concrete in Denmark, and the air content between the different mixes differ to a smaller
extent. In conclusion further compressive strength could potentially have been gained had the speciﬁc air
content of each concrete mix been taken into account. However, in this case the effect of the air content
on the water to cement ratio is small due to the minor deviation from the air content used, and therefore it
is considered reasonable to not prioritize creating test batches for the mix designs in order to include the
actual air content.
Finally, it is observed that the compressive tests are successfully carried out seeing that the failure shapes
were all expected, and the friction angles were within a sensible range. Additionally, the stress-strain rela-
tions for the different concrete mixes are to a great extent in compliance with theory. Thus, it is concluded
37
==PAGE:196==
that achieving a desired strength of concrete while replacing natural aggregates in new concrete is possible
on the condition that the physical properties of the recycled concrete aggregates are taken into account.
38
==PAGE:197==
