Cementindhold i beton 
A 
Princip 
Betonprøven knuses og cementpastaen opløses i salpetersyre. Alle chlorider vil 
Herefter være opløst. Uopløselige dele filtreres fra, og mængden af chlorid i 
væskefasen bestemmes ved titrering med sølvnitrat. 
Metoden bestemmer ikke på hvilken form chloriden findes i betonprøven. Den 
siger ikke, om chloriden findes som natriumchlorid (almindelig salt), 
calciumchlorid eller andre chlorider. 
B 
Specielt apparatur 
Titrator 716 DMS Titrino 
C 
Kemikalie sikkerhed 
Salpetersyre - Brandnærende; Ætsende; Brandfarlig ved kontakt med 
brandbare stoffer. Alvorlig ætsningfare. Undgå indånding af dampe. Brug 
syrehandsker, plastikforklæder, sikkerhedsbriller og stinkskab ved afmåling. 
Læs kemikaliebrugsanvisningen før arbejdet begynder. 
D 
Reagenser 
Salpetersyre 1% HNO3: 
17 mL koncentreret HNO3 overføres med måleglas til en 1000,00 mL målekolbe 
som er ½ fyldt med destilleret vand. Der blandes godt og tilsættes vand til 
mærket. Efter blanding overføres opløsningen til en plastikflaske og mærkes. 
E 
Analysens udførelse 
50 g tørret knust prøve afvejes på teknisk vægt til et 1000L bægerglas. Der 
tilsættes ca. 200 mL varmt destilleret vand og det blandes.  
Derefter tilsættes der langsomt 3 x 10 mL konc. HNO3 til opslemningen som 
derefter blandes godt og stilles til afkøling til stuetemperatur (skal foregå i 
stinkskab). 
Sektionen for Materialer og Holdbarhed, F2020
B.D 2
==PAGE:209==
Der tilsættes ca. 10mL konc. HNO3 for at kontrollerer at alt materiale er opløst 
(luftudvikling). Fortsæt med at tilsætte HNO3 indtil der ikke er mere luftudvikling. 
Filtrer opløsningen gennem alm filter ned i et bægerglas. Skyl filtreret en gang 
med 1% HNO3 og derefter med destilleret vand 
Overfør opløsningen til en 500 ml målekolbe og fyld op til mærket med destilleret 
vand.  
Udtage 20 ml til måling af K+Na på ICP 
Chlorid titrer med resten af prøven – se vejledning for chlorid titrering 
F 
Affaldshåndtering 
Ekstrakerne hældes i affaldsdunk mærket X 4.41 (tungmetaller). 
Filterpapiret bortkastes i skraldespanden i stinkskabet.

1

Introduction
This annex is compiled to determine and compare the failure load of beams casted with recycled concrete
aggregates, RCA, and natural aggregates, NA, in reference to the project Combined Impacts for Beams
Casted with Fine and Coarse Recycled Concrete Aggregates. The beams are casted with two different con-
crete mixes that have a distribution of 18% water, 8% cement, and 74% aggregates, where the distribution of
aggregates in both mixes are 60% of the coarse fraction and 40% of the ﬁne fraction. For the beams casted
with RCA the coarse recycled concrete aggregates, C-RCA, constitute 100% of the coarse fraction, whereas
50% of the ﬁne fraction is replaced with ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA. This concrete mix is
referred to as F-RCA50, where the naming solely focuses on the replacement ratio of the ﬁne fraction due
to the entire coarse fraction being replaced with C-RCA. In order to determine the capacity of beams casted
with RCA, three different test set-ups are investigated to determine the behaviour of beams exposed to
combined impacts. The performance of these beams is compared to the performance of beams casted with
only natural aggregates, NA, which is referred to as F-NA40, where the naming focuses on the distribution
between coarse and ﬁne aggregates seeing that all aggregates in this mix are natural. The concrete mixes
have a desired compression strength of 20MPa and are considered exposed to the passive environmental
class. All beams tested are reinforced with the same amount and distribution of reinforcement.
The current annex will describe the methods used to cast the beams, where the workability and the air
content of each batch is investigated. Furthermore, the strength of the reinforcement and the ultimate load
of the beams for each test set-up is determined. Table 1.1 summarizes which tests are carried out according
to their speciﬁc European Standards. For each test carried out the purpose, experimental procedure, results,
data processing, and potential sources of error are described, and ﬁnally a conclusion will summarize the
overall ﬁndings.
Table 1.1: Overview of experiments
Laboratory test
International Standard
Metallic Materials - Tensile Testing

DS/EN 12350-2
Air Content
DS/EN 12350-7
Compressive strength

==PAGE:236==
2

Yield Strength of Reinforcement
Upon designing structural elements composed of reinforced concrete the advantages of the compressive
strength of the concrete and the tensile strength of the reinforcement are utilized. Reinforcement bars and
stirrups used to reinforce the beams in the current project have been provided by Lemvigh Müller and are
all ribbed steels of a quality corresponding to 550B, and in accordance with DS/EN 10080 [2]. B refers to
the ductility class, where B is recommended for structures veriﬁed using plasticity theory, and 550 refers
to a yield strength of 550MPa. Thus, the steels provided are known to have a yield strength of minimum
550MPa, and the purpose of the tensile test is to determine the actual yield strength of the rebars used, in
order to take this into account during veriﬁcation of the beams. The yield strength is determined from the
tensile test knowing that adequate ductility of the steel is deﬁned by the ratio of tensile strength to the yield
strength given in DS/EN 1992-1-1 [6].
2.1
Experimental Procedure
The longitudinal rebars used to reinforce the beams with regard to tensile and compression forces are of
diameters 8mm and 6mm respectively. For the tensile reinforcement the ultimate strength is determined
by performing ﬁve tensile tests using an Instron-8502 servo hydraulic test machine in compliance with the
set-up shown in Figure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Tensile test set-up
2
==PAGE:237==
Before being able to test the ﬁve tensile rebars they are cut into specimens with a length of 300mm using
an angle grinder. Subsequently, they are named SS1-SS5, measured, and weighed, and the speciﬁc cross
sectional area of each specimen is determined. Usually, the clamps belonging to the servo hydraulic test
machine cannot grip test specimens with diameters smaller than 10mm. However, this is dealt with by
positioning soft metallic sheets with a thickness of 2.5mm between the clamps and each test specimen.
Unfortunately, this is a limitation for the compressive reinforcement as it is not possible to create a proper
grip for the clamps with the metallic sheets available at the testing facility at DTU. Thus, the ultimate
strength is determined only for the compressive reinforcement, where the tensile test provides the force
used to rupture each test specimen.
The cross sectional area is determined by

From the ultimate strength the yield strength is determined by the ratio of tensile strength to the yield

fy = fu

2.4
Errors
Small uncertainties of the results can be related to the weighing and measurement of the test specimens.
These are considered relatively small and with a minor inﬂuence on the results.
2.5
Discussion
Upon calculation of the structural capacity of the beams in the current project it is assumed that the com-
pressive reinforcement has a yield strength corresponding to the yield strength of the tensile reinforcement,
which is not possible to corroborate due to the circumstances of the test machinery available at DTU. Fur-
thermore, the same yield strength is assumed applicable for the stirrups as these are of the same diameter
as the compressive reinforcement. These assumptions are considered reasonable seeing that the same man-
ufacturer provided all reinforcement.
Moreover, it is advantageous to determine the yield strength from the actual test rather than the relation
between the yield and tensile strength. However, as this is not possible due to the test set-up it is relevant
to mention that the ratio between the yield and tensile strength is not necessarily 1.08 as this is a minimum
requirement to obtain sufﬁcient ductility for steel grade B.
2.6
Partial Conclusion
As manufacturers have to supply reinforcement with a minimum yield strength it is expected that the actual
yield strength is higher than stated in the order description which makes the results credible. However,
not being able to take the actual yield strength of the compressive reinforcement and stirrups into account
during calculations may cause deviations between the test results.
5
==PAGE:240==
3
|
Casting of Beams
To evaluate the behaviour of beams casted with concrete mix F-RCA50, nine beams are casted. Addition-
ally, four beams are casted using concrete mix F-NA40 and used as a reference, when evaluating the beams
casted with RCA. For each beam an appertaining cylinder is casted in order to determine the compressive
strength of the concrete at the day of the testing. During casting of the beams the slump and the air content
of the fresh concrete batches are determined, and these values are evaluated and compared to the values
found in Annex B.
The geometry of each beam is given by a width of 125mm, a height of 250mm, and a length of 2m.
Each beam is reinforced with tensile and compressive longitudinal rebars with diameters of 8mm and 6mm
respectively. Furthermore, shear reinforcement is applied with a diameter of 6mm and a distance of 150mm
between the stirrups. Figure 3.1 shows an overview of the beam dimensions along with the cross sections.
Figure 3.1: Overview of beam geometry
The stirrups provided for the beams have a circular shape, which forces the longitudional rebars to be placed
further into the cross section than what is common practice, as shown in 3.1. The concrete cover of the
longitudional rebars is therefore 60mm, whereas the distances from the concrete surface to the stirrups are
35mm along both sides, top, and bottom of each beam. These concrete covers are larger than the required
minimum distances given in DS/EN 1992-1-1 [6] and are thereby in compliance with the current standard.
6
==PAGE:241==
3.1
Experimental Procedure
Prior to the casting, a reinforcement cage for each beam is made by tying the longitudinal rebars to the
stirrups. The reinforcement cage is placed in the formwork with reinforcement spacers of 35mm to ensure
the concrete cover of the stirrups. Figure 3.2 shows the reinforcement cage along with the cage placed in
the formwork.
(a) Stirrups tied to longitudinal rebars, spacing 150mm
(b) Reinforcement in formwork
Figure 3.2: Preparation for casting of beams
The reference beams are casted with concrete mix design F-NA40, consisting of an aggregate distribution
where 40% belongs to the ﬁne fraction and 60% to the coarse fraction. The beams casted with RCA are
made from concrete mix F-RCA50 consisting of an equal distribution between ﬁne fractions of RCA and
NA and 100% RCA for the coarse fraction. An overview of the two mix designs is shown in ﬁgure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: Overview of mix designs
7
==PAGE:242==
For each beam a concrete batch of 85 litres is mixed and used to cast the beam, the appertaining cylinder
and for testing the slump and the air content of each batch. The concrete mixes are made using the Eirich
mixing system at the structural lab at DTU, which consists of a number of silos containing aggregates and a
concrete mixer. This system automatically doses the neccessary aggregates, which are subsequently mixed
in the mixer with a rotation of 7m/s. Next, the fresh concrete is poured into the formwork after which the
beam is compacted with a rod vibrator, and additional concrete is added if needed. Lastly, two suspension
points are placed in the fresh concrete for transportation of the casted beams. After each beam is casted a
slump test is executed according to DS/EN 12350-2 [3], to which a detailed description is found in Annex
B. Next, the air content of the batch is determined according to DS/EN 12350-7 [4]. Finally the appertaining
cylinder with a height of 200mm and a diameter of 100mm is casted according to the procedure in DS/EN
12390-1:2 [5]. Then each beam is stored in the structural lab underneath a plastic cover for 24 hours, after
which the beams and the appertaining cylinders are removed from the formwork and stored in curing water
until the day of the test. This procedure is repeated for each of the beams casted.
3.2
Results
For each batch the slump and the air content are determined, and below Table 3.1 and 3.2 shows the recorded
slumps and the air content for each batch.

3.4
Errors
The casting of beams follows the same procedure for both the tying of reinforcement and the casting of
each beam and cylinder. The same equipment is used and the same person has been assigned to the same
processes and tasks upon every casting. This is done to ensure that the beams are casted as similarly as
possible and under uniform conditions. Although this is kept in mind errors throughout the execution are
unavoidable.
Errors related to the reinforcement tying are apparent due to the longitudinal rebars being skew, which leads
to skew cages and thereby to varying distances between reinforcement and formwork. Figure 4.19a shows
a beam where a straight reinforcement cage is placed in the formwork, whereas Figure 4.19b shows one of
the more skew cages.
(a) Straight reinforcement cage
(b) Skew reinforcement cage
(c) Imprecise position of rebars
Figure 3.4: Errors related to the reinforcement
9
==PAGE:244==
Another error related to the reinforcement is the imprecise placement of the longitudinal rebars caused by
the circular shape of the stirrups seeing that it is somewhat difﬁcult to place the longitudinal reinforcement
at the exact same position each time. This error results in an uneven distance between the rebars and the
formwork, which is shown in Figure 3.4c.
Errors related to the concrete mixes are considered insigniﬁcant due to the use of the Eirich mixing system.
The system is weighing and dosing the aggregates automatically, according to the desired mix design. How-
ever, errors related to uneven compaction of the concrete are unavoidable. The fresh concrete is vibrated
until no air bobbles are apparent on the surface, which is hard to do uniformly for every beam as this is
a visual evaluation. Too little vibration may lead to a concrete with a large amount of air bobbles which
weakens the beam. Contrarily, concrete that is vibrated too much may separate.
3.5
Discussion
When considering the slumps found for the beams casted with mix design F-RCA50 and the beams casted
with mix design F-NA40, it is apparent that the slumps in general are within the desired range of 60-200mm
[1] which is applicable for concrete in practical use. However one batch, BB6, is found to have a slump
below this range of 50mm, which may be due to the time of the test. Both beam types have similar slump
measures which is underlined by the fact that the mean slump is 80mm in both cases.
The found slumps are evaluated in correlation to the slumps found in Annex B, Table 7.1, which were 70mm
for mix design F-NA40 and 80mm for mix design F-RCA50. Thus, the mean slumps found for the beams
of F-RCA50 are equal to the value found in Annex B, Table 7.1. For the beams casted with F-NA40 the
mean slump is found to be slightly higher than the slump of 70mm found in Annex B, Table 7.1. However,
it should be kept in mind that only one slump test was executed for F-NA40 and F-RCA50 respectively in
Annex B, Table 7.1. Another factor that could affect the comparison is the use of different mixing machines
with different rotation velocities.
From the air content measurements it is apparent that the air content in the beams made from mix design
F-RCA50 are lower than the values found for the F-NA40 beams. The mean air content for the F-RCA50
beams is 1.0% where the F-NA40 beams has a mean of 1.4%, which is within the traditional range of 1-2%
[7]. Both values are lower than the values found in Annex B, Table 8.1 where the values were 1.5% and
1.6% for F-RCA50 and F-NA40 respectively. As for the slump the comparison is used as an indication of
how consistent the concrete can be made. The comparison shows that it is hard to make the exact same
concrete mix several times. However the change of machinery should be kept in mind.
10
==PAGE:245==
3.6
Partial Conclusion
The beams are casted as uniformly as possible to ensure consistent conditions for the experiments. Errors
related to the reinforcement are noted in order to track potential errors if necessary. The concrete mixes
are overall found to be applicable for casting based on the found slumps and air contents. The comparison
between the slump test and the air content values and the results found in Annex B Table 7.1 and 8.1 show
that the mixes have very similar properties, despite the different mixing procedures.
11
==PAGE:246==
4
|
Testing of Beams
The beams are tested in three different test set-ups, two where the beams are tested in bending and shear
with different shear spans, and one where the beams are exposed to combined bending, shear, and torsion.
Each test results in a force-displacement curve that provides the failure load of the test specimen, and for
each of the test set-ups one F-NA40 reference beam is tested alongside three F-RCA50 beams casted with
RCA.
4.1
Experimental Procedure
As preparation for the beam experiments the beams and the appertaining cylinders are removed from the
curing tub and lightly cleansed with water in order to remove residual ﬁlth and sludge. While waiting for the
beams to dry the compressive strength of each cylinder is determined in accordance with DS/EN 12390-2
[5]. One side of each beam is painted white in order to ensure good visibility of the cracks that will occur
during the tests. Once each beam is dry it is carefully placed in the test machine.
The testing of each beam is carried out using an Instron-6025 electro mechanical test machine, which is
functioning as a frame that applies force by means of a linear actuator, after which the force is distributed
into two point loads due to a yoke being attached to the frame and the load cell as shown in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Test set-up using Instron-6025
12
==PAGE:247==
A data-logger is connected to the test machine and receives input from the load cell and six deformation
measurement devices mounted to the beam. For all beams the test duration is aimed towards being approxi-
mately 25 minutes, and upon loading of each beam cracking patterns are visually inspected and emphasized
with makers. Upon reaching the ultimate load of each beam the test machine is stopped and the results are
recorded.
Throughout the beam experiments three different test conﬁgurations are used in an attempt to investigate
the behaviour of the beams at several positions of the yield condition; two of them only exposing the beams
to bending and shear and the ﬁnal one exposing the beams to bending, shear, and torsion simultaneously.
The following Figure 4.2 shows the ﬁrst test conﬁguration, VM1, in which the shear span is 400mm. Due
to limitations of the geometry of the yoke that distributes the load from the load cell into two point loads
affecting the beam this is the smallest shear span used in the testing of the beams.
Figure 4.2: Test set-up for VM1
The beams tested in this conﬁguration are apparent from Table 4.1 below, where the reference identiﬁcation
speciﬁes which beams are tested, and the notation name speciﬁes which naming convention is used during
data treatment for each beam.
Table 4.1: Beams used for testing of conﬁguration VM1

600mm thus resulting in a greater bending moment.

to the one used in VM1 of 400mm. However, in this test conﬁguration the load is applied eccentrically,
which results in a torsional moment affecting the beam.
Figure 4.4: Test set-up for VMT

==PAGE:249==
Seeing that the load cell cannot be exposed to torsion two devices have been customised to enable the
eccentricity of the point loads. These devices are fabricated from the drawings shown in Figure 4.5, and
they are attached directly to the concrete beams with an interlayer of 1cm masonite.
Figure 4.5: Fabrication drawing for torsional device, measurements given in mm
In this load conﬁguration the concrete beams are placed eccentrically in the test machine with the torsional
devices on top, after which the yoke is placed on the torsional devices with the centre line of the yoke
and load cell being directly above the ball bearing, thus 20mm away from the centre line of the concrete
beam. Below, Figure 4.6 shows the torsional devices. Note, that the welds from the fabrication drawing are
replaced by bolts.
(a) Torsional device with ball bearing (b) Plate for transfer of load from yoke
Figure 4.6: Device fabricated to enable torsional loading
15
==PAGE:250==
For each of the test conﬁgurations six deformation measurement devices are placed on the beams as shown
in Figures 4.7 and 4.8. These are connected to the data-logger and will provide the deformations occurring
at the supports and by the load during testing.
Figure 4.7: Placement of deformation measurement devices for VM1 and VM2
Figure 4.8: Placement of deformation measurement devices for VMT
4.2
Results
The diameters of the appertaining cylinders and the ultimate loads found from the compression tests are
documented and used to determine the compressive strength of the concrete on the day of the experiment.
The compressive strengths of the cylinders are considered equal to the concrete strength of each beam. The
compressive strengths along with other essential parameters can be found in Appendix A, where the data is
listed for each beam speciﬁcally.
The cross sectional dimensions of the beams are measured and the surfaces are visually inspected. During
testing the cracks are highlighted and documented as these form the basis of evaluating what kind of failure
has ruptured each of the beams. Below, Figures 4.9, 4.10, 4.11, and 4.12 show the beams after testing. Note,
that VM1-B-4 is documented separately due to circumstances during testing, which will be elaborated in
Section 4.3.
16
==PAGE:251==
Figure 4.9: VM1-B-1, VM1-B-2, VM1-B-3, and VM1-B-R after testing
Figure 4.10: VM2-B-1, VM2-B-2, VM2-B-3, and VM2-B-R after testing
17
==PAGE:252==
Figure 4.11: VMT-B-1, VMT-B-2, VMT-B-3, and VMT-B-R after testing
Figure 4.12: VM1-B-4 after testing
4.3
Data Processing
In order to interpret the data received by the data-logger from each of the tests carried out a calibration of
each of the six deformation measurement devices has been performed, and this done in a manner corre-
sponding to the calibration described in Annex B. In this case the migration distances and electrical signals
are acquired for data points with approximately 5mm between them, and from this linear relations are re-
sulting in the trend lines shown in Figure 4.13 for device H1, where the remaining trend lines are shown in
Appendix B, Figures C.B.1 and C.B.2.
18
==PAGE:253==
Figure 4.13: Trend line for calibration of measuring device H1
Knowing an expression for the trend line of each measurement device allows for the displacement to be
calculated from the data stored with the data-logger. In a similar manner the force from the load cell
is determined from the received signals of the test machine seeing that this supplies 100kN/V. In order
to obtain a force-displacement curve that provides a reasonable base of comparison between the beams
tested within each load conﬁguration, the displacement used in the curves is the vertical deformation at
the load in the side of the beam where failure occurs. This choice is justiﬁed upon evaluating the different
displacements obtained for the reference beam, VM1-B-R, tested in conﬁguration VM1 which is apparent
in Figure 4.14.
Figure 4.14: Recording of displacements for VM1-B-R
19
==PAGE:254==
From the ﬁgure above it is clearly visible that both the vertical and horizontal deformations at the support
are signiﬁcantly smaller than the vertical deformations by the loads, which is to be expected. Furthermore,
it is apparent from the ﬁgure that the failure of the beam occurs at the left side of the beam corresponding
to the force-displacement curve named VM1-B-R-V1, seeing that this deformation develops notably more
upon reaching the failure load. Therefore, the displacement obtained at the side of the failure is used to
depict the force-displacement curves throughout the processing of data that follows below. A similar plot
is made for test conﬁguration VMT to ensure that no unexpected displacements are occurring and that the
same tendency appears. The plot is shown in Appendix B, Figure C.B.3.
During testing of conﬁguration VM1 it is observed that the beams fail in bending despite some shear cracks
being visible. Furthermore, a failure load between 60kN and 70kN is recorded for all beams tested in this
conﬁguration, which is apparent from Figure 4.15, where it is also found that the reference beam, VM1-B-R,
has the largest capacity. Additionally, all beams have a similar behaviour and are in fairly good compliance
throughout the experiments for VM1, despite a slightly lower stiffness being observed for beam VM1-B-2
than for the remaining beams.
Figure 4.15: Force-displacement curve for VM1
Before deciding on a shear span of 600mm for test conﬁguration VM2 an option of investigating the beams
with a shorter shear span of 300mm was tested on one beam, which is the reason that only two beams with
F-RCA50 are tested in VM2. The force-displacement curves for the two beams casted with F-RCA50 and
the reference beam casted with F-NA40 are illustrated in Figure 4.16.
20
==PAGE:255==
Figure 4.16: Force-displacement curve for VM2
The shorter shear span of 300mm was tested on one beam which did not fail within the capacity of the
test machine, seeing that the decrease in bending moment resulted in a signiﬁcant increase in the force
needed to induce failure of the beam. Thus, seeing that the test facilities at DTU could not accommodate
such loading, a shear span of 600mm was decided upon for test conﬁguration VM2. From Figure 4.16
it is apparent that the failure load is between 40kN and 50kN, which is signiﬁcantly lower than observed
for conﬁguration VM1. This is caused by the increase in bending moment, and again it is observed that
the reference beam, VM2-B-R, has a slightly larger capacity compared to the beams casted with RCA.
Furthermore, the deviation between the behavior of the beams is very small for test conﬁguration VM2.
Next, for the test conﬁguration VMT the failure loads observed are approximately between 60kN and 70kN
as for the case of VM1, which is expected due to the shear span yet again being 400mm. However, in this
case it is noted that deformations develop signiﬁcantly faster than for conﬁguration VM1, which entails that
the torsional stiffness is somewhat smaller than the bending stiffness of the beams. Again, the behavior of
the beams is quite aligned, despite larger deviations being observed for test conﬁguration VMT than for the
remaining conﬁgurations VM1 and VM2.
21
==PAGE:256==
Figure 4.17: Force-displacement curve for VMT
Finally, Figure 4.18 shows the force-displacement curve for the beam, that did not fail within the capacity
of the test machine in test conﬁguration VM2. This beam is evaluated separately due to its load history
being different than for the remaining beam tests. Upon the capacity failure of the test machine the beam
was unloaded, which caused crack propagation to stop and some cracks to contract. Afterwards, the beam
was brought to failure using test conﬁguration VM1, where it reached a failure load in correspondence with
the ones recorded for the remaining beams of VM1 as apparent in Figure 4.18, where it is referred to as
VM1-B-4. Thus, it seems that the initial loading did not affect the capacity of the beam.
Figure 4.18: Force-displacement curve for VM1-B-4
22
==PAGE:257==

ent in the table corresponding to conﬁguration VM1. Finally, the mean values and standard deviations are
determined for the beams casted with F-RCA50 in each of the test conﬁgurations.

4.4
Errors
An error that could affect the results is related to the surface of the beam and the steel yoke that applies the
force to the upper surface of the beam. Since the beam surface is not totally plane the yoke and the attached
steel rollers will apply the load unevenly due to the irregular concrete surface. This will in some cases mean
that the load is mainly applied to some speciﬁc parts of the beam instead of evenly throughout the cross
section. In Figure 4.19a it is illustrated how the load ought to be distributed, whereas the issue related to the
23
==PAGE:258==
uneven surface of the concrete beam is depicted in Figure 4.19b. Similarly, it can be a challenge to place
the yoke exactly in the centre line of the beam, which may introduce eccentricities that can cause small
torsional moments unintentionally. This is apparent from the Figures 4.19c and 4.19d.
(a) Even distribution
(b) Uneven distribution
(c) Centered load
(d) Eccentric load
Figure 4.19: Errors related to test set-up
Another source of error that may inﬂuence the results of the experiments is caused by the placement of the
supports, as it is difﬁcult to place the beams without moving the supports slightly. However, this movement
is considered very small.
The position of the suspension points introduce challenges as these are sometimes coinciding with the
yoke once the deﬂection of the concrete beams increase. A solution to this was bending the suspension
points by pounding them with a large hammer before placing the beams in the test machine. However, this
might introduce some micro cracks and thereby weaken the cross section, which is not taken into account.
Unfortunately, no other options were possible without the transportation and lifting of the beams being
problematic and unsafe. Another source of error related to the suspension points is found in some of the
beam tests where the increasing deformations causes the yoke to push down on the suspension points, thus
introducing an additional point load. Although this is observed the source of error is considered of minor
inﬂuence due to it occurring very close to failure. Hence, this minor point load does not really contribute to
the failure, as cracks are already heavily propagated at this point.
24
==PAGE:259==
4.5
Discussion
From the beam experiments it is apparent that the behavior of the beams are in better coherence upon
increasing the bending moment, which is indicated by the small deviations registered for VM2, where
bending is clearly dominant. Thus, it seems that an increase in shear stresses causes larger deviations in the
structural behavior of the test specimens, as both shear and torsion contribute to the shear stresses.
In all test conﬁgurations it is apparent that bending dictates the failure of the beams as ﬂexural cracks
transpire initially, and the development of shear cracks is marginal in most cases. Nonetheless, it is observed
that the ﬂexural cracks and the shear cracks coincide. The sparse appearance of shear cracks indicates that
the shear reinforcement stirrups are placed too closely, which is difﬁcult to avoid as it is preferable that the
reinforcement cage has a certain stiffness.
Furthermore, small deformations are registered by the supports of the beams, and theoretically these ought
to be zero in the case of VM1 and VM2. However, this is very problematic to ensure with the given test set-
up. Finally, the amount of days in curing are varying for the beams casted with F-RCA50 and the reference
beams casted with F-NA40. As the beams casted with F-NA40 have shorter curing periods the deviation
recorded between the failure loads may be underestimated.
4.6
Partial Conclusion
The standard deviations recorded between the beams casted with F-RCA50 are relatively small for all test
conﬁgurations, and additionally the difference between the mean values of the F-RCA50 beams is very
small in comparison with the results obtained for the reference F-NA40 beams being below 5kN for all test
conﬁgurations.
25
==PAGE:260==
5
|
Conclusion
As a result of the experimental work all failure loads are found to be within intervals of less than 10kN for
each load conﬁguration, which is considered a reasonable deviation upon recognizing uncertainties related
to factors such as vibration, skewness of reinforcement and the position of the test set-up. Furthermore, for
all test conﬁgurations it is observed that the reference beams casted with F-NA40 all have slightly larger
structural capacity than the beams casted with F-RCA50.
Another conclusion of the experimental work is that the coherence between the behaviour of the beams
casted with F-NA40 and F-RCA50 respectively is very good, and in this regard the deviations are considered
minimal. This observation is apparent for all test conﬁgurations, and thus the cracking tendencies are similar
to those known for regular concrete, which underlines that concrete casted with recycled aggregates is a
reliable and useful alternative to regular concrete.

1

Introduction
The current annex is created to investigate the relative environmental footprint of different concrete mixes
containing varying ratios of recycled concrete aggregates, RCA, as part of the project Combined Impacts
for Beams casted with Fine and Coarse Recycled Concrete Aggregates.
In general sustainable development is most commonly deﬁned using three main parameters, namely social
sustainability, economic sustainability, and environmental sustainability. With regard to the built environ-
ment social sustainability is hard to quantify as this refers to the relation between users and their surround-
ings such as the amount of daylight, security, and acoustics that affect the user’s well-being in a building.
Next, economic sustainability focuses on the balance between the costs of a building and the quality of said
building in which the entire lifetime of the building is evaluated. Finally, environmental sustainability refers
to the environmental footprint of a building in a local, regional, and global perspective [1].
Evaluation of the environmental sustainability of a material or a building is most often carried out in accor-
dance with current standards such as DS/EN ISO 14040, DS/EN ISO 14044, and DS/EN 15978. However,
these do not take social and economical sustainability into account. Besides from the standards the use of
certiﬁcation schemes such as Deutsche Gessellschaft für Nachhaltiges Bauen, DGNB, and Svanemærket
is steadily growing in building industry, and these seek to incorporate all three branches of sustainability.
Also, the use of these certiﬁcations increases the uniformity on how to handle the assessment of the envi-
ronmental, social, and economical sustainability of materials and buildings, which secures comparability
and transparency of products across the industry.
Upon carrying out a life cycle assessment, LCA, the amount of data available on speciﬁc materials often
poses challenges as it is still a relatively new task in the building industry. In order to increase the environ-
mental assessment of materials a growing number of suppliers and companies are publishing environmental
product declarations, EPDs, which deﬁne the environmental properties of a speciﬁc product according to
DS/EN 15804.
Generally, an LCA consists of ﬁve stages that describe the entire life cycle of a given system, and these are
further subdivided into processes related to the speciﬁc stages in accordance with DS/EN 15978 as shown
in Figure 1.1. In order to assess the sustainability of a product system it is important to take its entire life
1
==PAGE:286==
cycle into account, as each life cycle stage may have different contributions to the overall sustainability of
the product system.
Figure 1.1: Overview of life cycle stages [2]
In the current annex the main principles of the phases of an LCA are used to investigate whether there is a
potential of reducing the environmental impact of concrete by replacing natural aggregates, NA, with RCA.
Thus, the focus of the LCA is directed towards environmental sustainability, and it seeks to evaluate whether
it is environmentally sound to use recycled concrete in the building industry. Below, Figure 1.2 illustrates
which replacement ratios of aggregates are investigated, where the total amount of aggregates constitute
74% of each concrete mix, and the distribution between ﬁne and coarse aggregates is 40% ﬁne aggregates
and 60% coarse aggregates. Similarly, the percentage distributions given below each concrete mix referred
to as F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 respectively, illuminate the ratios between NA and
RCA in each mix design.
Figure 1.2: Overview of mix designs
2
==PAGE:287==
The basis of the LCA study is carried out according to the standards given in Table 1.1. However, these
are quite comprehensive, and therefore only the sections relevant for the current project are taken into
consideration. Thus, this LCA study cannot be considered a full LCA as the scope is delimited.
Table 1.1: Overview of LCA Guidelines
Laboratory test
European Standard
Life cycle assessment, principles and framework
DS/EN ISO 14040
Life cycle assessment, requirements and guidelines
DS/EN ISO 14044
Sustainability of construction works
DS/EN 15978
Furthermore, as the scope of the LCA study limited, the results are to be considered guiding and not as
documentation, seeing that uncertainties arise due to a shortcoming of data available and assumptions made
in the completion of the LCA study.
The present report is structured in such a manner that the goal and scope of the LCA are stated and elabo-
rated, after which the modelling procedure of the product system considered is described in detail. Finally, a
life cycle impact assessment, LCIA, will state the overall ﬁndings of the LCA with regard to different impact
categories, and a life cycle interpretation, LCI, will conclude on the potential of reducing the environmental
footprint of concrete by replacing NA with RCA.
3
==PAGE:288==
2
|
Goal and Scope
2.1
Goal Deﬁnition
The intention of the current LCA is to investigate the environmental footprint of a traditional concrete mix
casted with NA, F-NA40, in comparison to three concrete mixes casted with RCA, F-RCA50, F-RCA80,
and F-RCA100 respectively. The LCA study is executed to investigate whether limiting the use of NA
will decrease the environmental footprint of structural concrete. For the current LCA the target audience
consists of engineers, architects, and entrepreneurs of the building industry as it aims to examine whether
casting concrete with RCA will increase the relative sustainability of structural concrete.
The current LCA is carried out as a comparative analysis and can therefore not conclude on the overall
sustainability of the investigated concrete mixes. However, it seeks to highlight whether a potential of
improvements with regard to environmental performance is present upon casting concrete with RCA, by
investigating the environmental footprint of concrete casted with different replacement ratios. Furthermore,
it seeks to examine how the use of RCA in new concrete affects the different impact categories that all
contribute to the overall environmental footprint of the concrete.
During the LCA study a number of assumptions are made to simplify the scope of the LCA, which is a
consequence of the short time frame available. Amongst others, these assumptions include considering data
from environmental product declarations, EPDs, regarding the cement and aggregates to be sufﬁcient in the
creation of the LCA.
2.2
Scope Deﬁnition
The current analysis is comparative, and therefore the function of the concrete is deﬁned to ensure that the
comparison is carried out on the same basis for each concrete mix. The function of structural concrete is to
provide a certain load carrying capacity when used in construction throughout the building industry. Thus,
the functional unit of the current LCA study is
1 m3 of concrete shall provide a compressive strength of at least 20MPa for a time frame of 50 years
4
==PAGE:289==
The entire product system for structural concrete is illustrated in Figure 2.1, where all the processes that
contribute to the environmental footprint are included. The dashed green lines indicate the transition be-
tween the different life cycle stages.
Figure 2.1: System boundary for the comparative LCA study
The product phase includes extraction of raw materials, transport, and production of structural materials.
Similarly, the building process uses the materials from the product phase and includes the transport and
assembly of these, whereas the use phase takes operation, maintenance, repairs, renovation, and energy
and water consumption into consideration. Finally, the end of life stage considers the demolition, trans-
port, incineration and disposal of the different components, whereas the life cycle stage outside the system
boundary is included to assess the potential for reuse and recycling of the products from the end of life
stage. As the building processes, the use phases, the end of life phases, and the potentials for recycling and
reuse outside the system boundaries are identical for the different concrete mixes considered, it is not rele-
vant to include these in the comparative LCA study. The system boundary is therefore limited to include the
processes that contribute to the product phase of the concrete mix, and the system boundary is marked with
a read line in Figure 2.1. This limitation is considered applicable since the differences between traditional
concrete and concrete casted with RCA are found in the product phase and due to the end of life scenarios
being presumed equivalent for both the traditional and the recycled concrete. However, it is well-known that
an environmental footprint is associated with the concrete throughout all life cycle stages. The reference
ﬂows are based on 1m3 of concrete in coherence with the functional unit.
5
==PAGE:290==
3
|
Modelling of Product System
The modelling of the LCA is carried out in LCAbyg version 5.2.1.0, which is a software tool developed by
BUILD - Department of The Build Environment, Aalborg University. The software is primarily applicable
for LCAs of buildings, which are based on DS/EN 15978 [5]. In LCAbyg the environmental impacts are
calculated throughout the entire lifetime of the building to be assessed as shown in Figure 1.1. However, in
the current project only the product phases A1-A3 referred to as cradle to gate are considered as highlighted
in Figure 3.1 below.
Figure 3.1: Life cycle phases according to the European standard
The product phase A1 consists of the gathering and extraction of raw materials, whereas phase A2 is the
transportation of raw materials to the production plant, and A3 is the production and manufacturing of the
product that will be used in the building process stage. During all processes of the product phase energy
is consumed, and excess waste needs to be eliminated. The phases A1-A3 are modelled for the cement,
natural aggregates, and recycled concrete aggregates by using EPDs that provide the environmental impacts
of the production of the raw materials.
The EPD for Rapid Cement is used to model the contributions from the cement, which corresponds to the
speciﬁc cement used in the project, and the environmental impacts of the EPD are found in Appendix A,
Table D.A.1. Similarly, the data used for the modelling of the NA are given in Appendix A, Table D.A.2,
which corresponds to marine aggregates manufactured by NCC Industry, Division of Stone Materials. The
EPD provides the environmental impacts for concrete sand with diameters of 0-4mm and for pebble with
sizes of 8-16mm which are both applicable for use in concrete mixes. Finally, the recycled concrete ag-
gregates are modelled by using an EPD corresponding to crushed aggregates applicable as road ﬁll, since
no EPD was found on the speciﬁc aggregates. The EPD for crushed aggregates as road ﬁll is assumed to
be a reasonable ﬁt for the modelling seeing that no data were possible to obtain from Norrecco A/S, who
provided the recycled concrete aggregates used in the current project. Furthermore, it is common practice to
6
==PAGE:291==
state only the most widely used application of the product on EPDs in general, and therefore it is not essen-
tial that the EPD only mentions the crushed concrete aggregates in relation to road ﬁll. The environmental
impacts of the EPD are found in Appendix A, Table D.A.3.
In the modelling of the concrete mixes the use of water is neglected due to the amounts of water being
constant in all mix designs, and seeing that LCAbyg is based on a library of building materials where no
data is present for the modelling of water. In contrast to this the cement content is taken into consideration
as this is known to have the largest share of the environmental footprint of concrete, despite of the cement
amounts also being constant. Seeing that only the product phases are included in the modelling of the
aggregates, this is also the case for the modelling of each concrete batch, where the cement and aggregates
correspond to the raw materials needed for production of concrete. Due to data given in the EPDs it is not
possible to distinguish between the phases A1-A3.
After the incorporation of the EPDs into LCAbyg the further modelling of the concrete mixes F-NA40,
F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100, respectively, is composed using the each of the mix recipes corre-
sponding to 1m3 as apparent in Table 3.1 and Table 3.2. These quantities are in coherence with the concrete
mixes designed in Annex B.
Table 3.1: Quantities of 1m3 concrete for the concrete mix casted with NA
Mix
Cement [kg/m3]
Sand [kg/m3]
Gravel, 4-8mm [kg/m3]

The analysis carried out in LCAbyg results in impact assessments distributed into seven different impact
categories that are apparent from Figure 3.2, where the unit for each category is presented along with a brief
description of the corresponding environmental issue. These impact categories form the basis of interpreting
the results of the LCA study, and the parameters measured are Global Warming Potential, GWP, Ozone
Depletion Potential, ODP, Photochemical Ozone Creation Potentials, POCP, Acidiﬁcation Potential, AP,
Eutrophication Potential, EP, Abiotic Depletion Potential elements, ADPe, and Abiotic Depletion Potential
fossil resources, ADPf.
7
==PAGE:292==
Figure 3.2: Impact categories with corresponding unit and environmental issue [2]
8
==PAGE:293==
4
|
Life Cycle Impact Assessment
Based on the modelling of the product system for the production of each concrete mix LCAbyg provides
the environmental impact of each mix distributed into the seven different impact categories. In an attempt to
corroborate the data obtained from the different EPDs for each aggregate type and the cement and to justify
the choices made in the modelling of the product system a comparison is made between F-NA40 and a
generic concrete with a compressive strength of 20MPa from the product library of LCAbyg. Below, Figure
4.1 shows the normalized results for each environmental impact category in a percentage-wise distribution
for the generic concrete mix and F-NA40 respectively.
Figure 4.1: Comparison of F-NA40 with generic concrete
In six out of seven impact categories the difference is less than 6%, and the greatest difference is found
for GWP where a deviation of 10% is observed. The deviations are assumed to be caused by the basis of
comparison not being fully identical, seeing that the generic concrete includes both transport and energy
used for the concrete production, whereas F-NA40 only considers the transport and energy consumption
during material extraction. For the generic concrete the distribution between the ﬁne and coarse aggregates
is unknown, which may also explain part of the deviations. Additionally, the energy sources are not known
for either of the concrete mixes, which may inﬂuence the comparison. Overall, the differences are con-
sidered minor which substantiates that the environmental footprint given in each EPD with regard to the
natural aggregates is reliable. Thus, the comparison indicates that the data from the EPDs used for the LCA
are representative.
9
==PAGE:294==
Usually, a sensitivity analysis is carried out to evaluate the robustness of the results obtained in an LCA
study, where a normalized sensitivity coefﬁcient is calculated after changing different parameters such as
quantities of materials or transportation distances. Provided that the sensitivity coefﬁcient is less than a
certain value deemed appropriate upon investigating a speciﬁc product system, the modelled system is
assumed not to be sensitive towards the parameter tested. Contrarily, if the sensitivity coefﬁcient is larger
than the appropriate value chosen, it is to be taken into account during the interpretation of the results of
the LCA. However, as the current study is based on information given in EPD’s the different processes that
go into the extraction of raw materials and crushing of concrete after demolition are not editable. Hence,
it is not possible nor relevant to carry out a sensitivity analysis of the modelled system, since the system
considered is limited to quite a narrow scope.
The results of the LCA study of the environmental footprint of the concrete mixes F-NA40, F-RCA50, F-
RCA80, and F-RCA100 respectively, which are based on EPD’s for the different aggregate types, are shown
below for all impact categories described in Section 3, and Figure 4.2. The results of the comparison of
the different concrete mixes for the two main contributors to the environmental footprint, namely GWP and
ADPf, are both decreased upon decreasing the amount of NA and replacing this with RCA. Note, that the
results are given for 1m3, and that the y-axis does not start at zero, which is intentional in order to emphasize
the differences between the concrete mixes. Had the y-axis started at zero the decreasing tendency of the
bars would not be visible.
Figure 4.2: Global warming potential and abiotic depletion potential for fossil resources for 1m3
10
==PAGE:295==
Next, a similar tendency is observed for the third and fourth largest contributors to the environmental foot-
print, namely AP and EP, which is apparent from Figure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Acidiﬁcation potential and eutrophication potential for 1m3
Contrarily, for the remaining three impact categories, ODP, POCP, and ADPe, which constitute approxi-
mately 5% of the total environmental footprint of the F-NA40 concrete mix, a decreasing amount of NA
and an increasing amount of RCA leads to enlarged values of the different impact categories, which is
apparent from Figures 4.4 and 4.5.
Figure 4.4: Ozone depletion potential and photochemical ozone creation potentials 1m3
11
==PAGE:296==
Figure 4.5: Abiotic depletion potential for elements for 1m3
The causes and effects of the results obtained above will be further investigated and elaborated in the life
cycle interpretation, LCI.
12
==PAGE:297==
5
|
Life Cycle Interpretation
From the impact assessment it is observed that four out of seven impact categories are higher for the concrete
mixture made of F-NA40 compared to the RCA mixtures. These impact categories are GWP, ADPf, AP,
and EP, which are also corresponding to the impact categories that have the largest contributions to the
overall environmental footprint of concrete. Based on these impact categories the potential of improving
the environmental footprint is large as emissions decrease upon an increase in the use of RCA. For ADPf
the difference between the F-NA40 mix and the mixes with RCA is not as large, however, the tendency is
still clear.
Adversely, it is observed that the remaining three impact categories ODP, POCP, and ADPe are increasing
for the RCA mixtures. Thus, these impact categories indicate that an increase in the use of RCA will
affect the environmental footprint negatively. Below, Table 5.1 highlights which of the concrete mixes F-
NA40 and F-RCA50 result in the highest and lowest emissions marked with red and green respectively.
Furthermore, it highlights the impact categories where the difference between the concrete mixes exhibits
a markedly different behaviour, and in the current project this is deﬁned as a factor 2 of difference, which
is current for the categories POCP and ADPe.
Table 5.1: Interpretation of results per impact category
Regarding the impact categories of POCP and ADPe these results are peculiar and unexpected, especially
seeing that ADPe concerns raw materials and minerals, and the use of recycled concrete aggregates ought
to decrease this impact category rather than increase it. The impact category POCP concerns photochemical
pollution which can be caused by factory emissions, and in this regard the increase is reasonable seeing that
the demolished concrete needs to be processed before reuse is possible. However, as the LCA study carried
out is comparative the beneﬁts of avoiding extraction of raw material will not be visible from the results.
In order to incorporate the effect of not extracting new raw material a consequentional LCA is necessary
13
==PAGE:298==
to conduct, seeing that such a type of LCA takes all systems and consequences into consideration in the
modelling of a speciﬁc scenario. Thus, the avoided amount of raw material would in that case have to be
credited in order to potentially be part of another product system and thereby affect the impact category of
ADPe positively.
Overall, the results of the LCA study are considered reliable due to the low complexity of the modelled
system, which decreases the risk of error upon modelling and due to the amounts of each component being
accurately determined and not estimated. However, as the only EPD used that is product-speciﬁc is the
one for Rapid Cement, uncertainties are introduced upon not having sufﬁcient data present for either of
the aggregate types. Therefore, the EPDs used for both NA and RCA are assumed to be applicable to the
product system, but this is not actually veriﬁable besides from the comparison carried out for NA with
regard to the concrete found in the generic library of LCAbyg.
Due to the circumstances regarding the LCA study it is not possible to obtain the actual environmental
footprint of the concrete mixes, as the results only provide an indication of how the concrete mixes behave
in relation to each other. This is due to the assumptions made upon determining the goal and scope of the
LCA, where the processes and life cycle stages that are identical for all concrete mixes are disregarded.
In conclusion the overall environmental footprint can be reduced upon replacing NA with RCA. However,
it is a limitation of the results that it has not been possible to corroborate the EPD used for speciﬁc RCA
used, as this could potentially provide a more accurate view of the environmental footprint.
14

1

Introduction
The current annex is created to further investigate the concrete mixes containing varying ratios of recycled
concrete aggregates, RCA, as part of the project Combined Impacts for Beams casted with Fine and Coarse
Recycled Concrete Aggregates.
Five different concrete mixes were made and investigated in Annex B, and in the following only four mixes
are presented due to the ﬁfth, F-NA50, being made as a preliminary test to determine the most suitable ratio
between ﬁne and coarse aggregates. The four different concrete mixes consist of one traditional concrete
mix with an aggregate distribution constituted by 40% of the ﬁne fraction and 60% of the coarse fraction.
This aggregate ratio is kept constant for the remaining mixes but with a 100% replacement of the coarse
fraction of natural aggregates, NA, with coarse recycled concrete aggregates, C-RCA. The ﬁne fraction of
NA is partially replaced with ﬁne recycled concrete aggregates, F-RCA, with replacement ratios of 50%,
80%, and 100%, for concrete mix F-RCA50, F-RCA80, and F-RCA100 respectively. The different concrete
mixtures and their speciﬁc shares of components are illustrated in Figure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Overview of mix designs
The compressive strengths of the different concrete mixes are apparent from Figure 1.2, where the strengths
are mean values based on four cylinders being exposed to uniaxial compression testing. From the bar plot
it is observed that the highest strength is found for the traditional concrete, F-NA40, whereas a tendency
1
==PAGE:312==
of decreasing strengths with increasing replacements ratios are apparent for the concrete mixes casted with
RCA.
Figure 1.2: Overview of compressive strengths for the concrete mixes after 28 days of hardening
Additionally, it is observed that concrete mix F-RCA80 has a signiﬁcantly lower compressive strength
compared to the remaining two concrete mixes casted with RCA. The observation is rather unexpected
seeing that concrete mix F-RCA100 contains a larger amount of RCA and has a higher strength than F-
RCA80 with a lower amount of RCA. These observations are the basis of the following annex as the
reasoning for the results are examined and sought determined.
In the following the potential causes of the low compressive strength of F-RCA80 are evaluated, and reasons
such as errors during the casting process, errors related to the concrete proportioning or errors caused by
potential over-vibration of the cylinders are investigated. Another reason could be related to the water
absorption of the F-RCA which is considered uncertain since the method used for the determination is
based on sand and not F-RCA.
Additionally, F-RCA100 is investigated further due to the unexpected high compressive strength in compar-
ison to F-RCA80. The general observation found for concrete casted with RCA is that a higher replacement
ratio lead to lower strengths, which is not the case when considering F-RCA80 and F-RCA100. Whether
the compressive strength of F-RCA100 is a coincidence is investigated by casting new cylinders with a
modiﬁed casting procedure.
2
==PAGE:313==
2
|
Segregation Check
During the casting process of concrete mix F-RCA80 in Annex B an observation raised concerns related to
the homogeneity of the mixture. The mixture appeared to be divided in the coarse fraction and the cement
paste as apparent from Figure 2.1a. Furthermore, during vibration, a rather odd behavior is observed in
comparison to the remaining mixes. Figure 2.1b shows how the cement paste splashes during vibration,
which makes it hard to determine whether the concrete is vibrated sufﬁciently.
(a) Concrete mix F-RCA80
(b) Concrete mix F-RCA80 while vibrating
Figure 2.1: Concrete mix F-RCA80 during the casting process
Based on the observations made an investigation of the homogeneity is conducted where the occurrence
of segregation is checked. This evaluation is done by a visual examination of a longitudinal cut through a
cylinder. The aim is to check whether the cylinder has been over-vibrated, which may cause the aggregates
to segregate.
(a) Section through cylinder
(b) Close-up of section
Figure 2.2: Section through cylinder F-RCA80
3
==PAGE:314==
Segregation is characterized by separation of the aggregate fractions, where large sized aggregates sink to
the bottom and cement paste is occuring in the upper part of the cylinder [4]. Figure 2.2b shows the sliced
cylinder along with a close-up of the aggregates. The cylinder section in Figure 2.2b shows no indication of
segregation as the aggregates are well-mixed which shows a rather homogeneous concrete mix. However,
when taking a closer look at the cylinder it is apparent that smaller voids of air are visible. Based on
this observation a more elaborate investigation is initiated where a ﬂuorescence impregnated thin section is
analyzed.
4
==PAGE:315==
3
|
Fluorescence Impregnated Thin Section
In order to obtain detailed knowledge about concrete mix F-RCA80 a ﬂuorescence impregnated thin section,
FITS, is made to investigate the micro-structural composition of the concrete mix. The aim of this analysis
is to investigate the homogeneity and air content of the mixture.
3.1
Experimental Procedure
One cylinder of concrete mix F-RCA80 is cut in half in the longitudinal direction as preparation for the
analysis, after which the cylinder is delivered to SEIR-materialeanalyse A/S who has provided the FITS.
The FITS is a 30mmx45mm section with a thickness of 20µm, and the FITS is apparent in Figure 3.1. The
analysis of the FITS is carried at DTU by allowing different types of light to penetrate the thin section and
thereby visually observe the characteristics through a microscope.
Figure 3.1: FITS of cylinder casted with F-RCA80
3.2
Results and Data Processing
During the examination different parts of the FITS are investigated and a number of observations are made.
The micro-structure of a selected area of the FITS is shown in Figure 3.2.
5
==PAGE:316==
(a) Enlarged area of FITS
(b) Enlarged area of FITS with highlights
Figure 3.2: Cut-out of FITS
Figure 3.4a shows an enlarged part of the FITS, where the aggregates, the cement paste and the air is
apparent. The cement paste is the light brown substance, whereas the yellow parts illustrate air. In Figure
3.4b the different aggregates are highlighted, where the blue solid lines show the RCA grains as these are
characterised by the dark brown cement paste. The grains highlighted with blue consist of a variety of
particles of different sizes which originate from the former concrete. Another type of grain is highlighted
with an orange dotted line, and these aggregates appear as single grains.
When comparing the air content of the cement paste, the light brown substance, with the old cement paste
around the RCA, the dark brown mass, it is apparent that the old cement paste contains small to no amount
of air in contrast to the light brown cement paste. The air content is further examined by taking a closer look
at a different part of the FITS. Figure 3.3 shows two different parts of the FITS where large amounts of air
is observed, seeing that the yellow colour indicates the presence of air. Note that the view set-up is different
compared to the view used for Figure 3.2 which is due to different microscopes being used throughout the
analysis.
(a) Area of FITS with large air content
(b) Another area of FITS with large air content
Figure 3.3: Cut-out of FITS with large air content
6
==PAGE:317==
From Figure 3.3 above it is clear that the concrete mix contains large amounts of air, which indicates that
the concrete mix was not vibrated sufﬁciently. The large air content increases the porosity of the concrete
due to the air ﬁlled voids. A high porosity results in a low compressive strength of the concrete, which
is in correlation with the observed behaviour of concrete mix F-RCA80. The large air content affects the
connection between the aggregates and the cement paste, which is weakened when air is found around the
aggregate grains. Figure 3.4 shows a cut-out of the FITS along with a close-up of the cut-out showing air
that is present in the perimeter of the grain which causes the grains to slip under when subjected to load.
(a) Overview of FITS with large air content
(b) Close-up of grain slip
Figure 3.4: Cut-out of FITS with grain slip
3.3
Errors
In the examination of the FITS only a small area is investigated which is assumed to be representative for
the entire concrete mix. However, this might not be the case.
3.4
Discussion
A wider investigation of the FITS would have been ideal to carry out along with several FITS’s made
on several cylinders of the same mixture, which could have minimised the uncertainties related to the
representativeness.
3.5
Partial Conclusion
The analysis of the FITS shows that the concrete mixture contains a large amount of air, which is probably
the reason behind the low compressive strength. It remains unknown why this tendency was only observed
for mix F-RCA80.
7
==PAGE:318==
4
|
Remix of F-RCA80
A remix of concrete mix F-RCA80 is executed to determine whether the low compressive strength is related
to an error in the mixing process or if the low strength is a general characteristic for the mix. The concrete
mix is remixed with the identical concrete mix design, however the mix design is corrected corresponding
to the water content of the aggregates at the day of the casting.
The water content is determined according to DS/EN 1097-5 [2], and a more detailed description of the
determination is apparent from the experimental procedure in Annex B, Section 4.1. The water content
is determined for both the C-RCA and the F-RCA due to the aggregates being stored outside and thereby
possibly containing larger amounts of water compared to the values found in Annex B. For the seasand the
value found in Annex B, Table 4.4 is used seeing that the same material with identical storage conditions
is used. The determination of water content is executed three times for each fraction with a mass of 1kg
for the coarse fraction and 0.5kg for the ﬁne fraction, the results is found in Appendix E.A Table E.A.1 and
E.A.2, whereas the mean water content and the standard deviation is apparent from Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Mean water content of the aggregates used for remixing of F-RCA80
Fraction
Mean Water Content [%]
Standard Deviation [-]
Seasand, 0-4mm
0.2
0.2
Fine Fraction, F-RCA
4.5
0.3
Coarse Fraction, C-RCA
0.6
2.6
The adjusted concrete mix design resulted in the quantities apparent from Table 4.2 which corresponds to a
batch of 30L. The full concrete mix design is found in Appendix E.B - Concrete Mix F-RCA80, Remix.
Table 4.2: Quantities for mix design F-RCA80
Mix
Cement [kg]
Water [L]
Sand [kg]
F-RCA [kg]
C-RCA [kg]
F-RCA80
7.1
7.3
4.5
13.4
32.2
8
==PAGE:319==
The quantities shown in the Table 4.2 above are poured into the concrete mixer, where the aggregates
are poured from largest to smallest particle size, after which the material is mixed. The mixing sequence
consists of mixing the dry material for three minutes, then adding water and mixing for three minutes,
pausing for three minutes, and ﬁnally remixing the materials for one minute. This lead to a rather wet
concrete mixture, with the same appearance as found for the original F-RCA80 concrete mix in Annex B.
In order to distinguish between the original F-RCA80 mix from Annex B and the mixes made for further
work in the current annex, the original mix is denoted F-RCA80-O in the remainder of the current annex.
The remixed F-RCA80 mixture and F-RCA80-O are shown in Figures 4.1a and 4.1b respectively.
(a) Current F-RCA80 concrete mix
(b) F-RCA80-O concrete mix
Figure 4.1: Current and original F-RCA80 concrete mix
From the concrete mixes shown in Figure 4.1 it is apparent that the mixes are both relatively wet with a clear
division between the large grains and the cement paste. Additionally, it is apparent that the water content in
the reproduced F-RCA80 mix is larger compared to the original mix. Based on this observation it is decided
not to carry on with casting of cylinders seeing that the texture of the mixture is similar to the original mix
and thereby similar results are assumed to be obtained. Furthermore, the observations indicate that the low
compressive strength obtained for F-RCA80-O was not due to an error in the mixing procedure.
9
==PAGE:320==
5
|
Summary
The separation check, the FITS analysis along with the remix of F-RCA80 showed that the mixture was
homogene but with a rather high air content, which resulted in a weak concrete mixture. From the remix
of F-RCA80 it is apparent that the low compressive strength is not related to an error during the mixing
procedure but rather an issue related to the water content of the mixture. The large water content is assumed
caused by the state of the aggregates and an overestimation of the water absorption coefﬁcient. The concrete
mixes are adjusted for the water absorption of the RCA which is relatively large for the F-RCA, with a value
of 24%. Based on the observations it is concluded to further investigate both concrete mixes F-RCA80 and
F-RCA100 since it is not certain whether F-RCA80 has an odd behaviour or if errors in concrete mix
F-RCA100 induced the larger compressive strength.
10
==PAGE:321==
6
|
Modiﬁed Mixing Procedure
In the following a modiﬁed mixing procedure compared to the one used in Annex B is introduced. The aim
is to examine whether an adjustment in the mixing procedure can inﬂuence the properties of the concrete
mixes, and thereby make it possible to obtain the desired compressive strength. In the procedure used in
Annex B the concrete mix designs are adjusted in relation to the water absorption coefﬁcient found for the
aggregates, which might have been overestimated and thereby leading to a high slump and low compressive
strength. This is further investigated by adjusting the mixing procedure where varying amounts of the water
absorption coefﬁcient for the F-RCA is used. The adjusted procedure is used for both concrete mix F-
RCA80 and F-RCA100 in order to determine whether the relation found for the compressive strengths has
been a coincidence. The modiﬁed mixing procedure consists of three batches for each mix design, the ﬁrst
one is conducted without considering the water absorption of F-RCA, in the next batch half of the water
absorption coefﬁcient is considered, which corresponds to 12%. Finally, the adjustment corresponds to the
total water absorption coefﬁcient of 24% for F-RCA corresponding to the value found in Annex B, section
3.3.
In the further investigation the water content is determined, which is used to establish the new concrete mix
designs. Furthermore, a particle size distribution is determined for each batch to document the exact distri-
bution of particles used in the different mixtures. Thereafter, the workability of the batches are examined
and ﬁnally two cylinders of each batch are casted.
6.1
Experimental Procedure, Results, and Data Processing
In order to avoid extensive repetition the results and data processing are combined in the following. Fur-
thermore, the experimental procedures are brieﬂy described with references to Annex B, where detailed
descriptions are found.
Water Content
Prior to the mixing procedure the aggregates are dried in an oven at 105°for 24 hours, after which constant
mass is ensured, the drying is executed in order to obtain uniform properties for the aggregates. At the day
of the casting the water content of the aggregates are determined in accordance with the procedure found in
Annex B, Section 4.1. The tests are executed three times for F-RCA and C-RCA respectively, after which
the water content is determined, ﬁnally the concrete mix designs are adjusted with the new values of water
11
==PAGE:322==
content. The mean values and the standard deviations are apparent from Table 6.1, for the speciﬁc results
see Appendix E.C. Tables E.C.1 and E.C.2.
Table 6.1: Mean water content of the aggregates used for the modiﬁed mixing procedure
Fraction
Mean Water Content [%]
Standard Deviation [-]
Fine Fraction, F-RCA
0.10
0.07
Coarse Fraction, C-RCA
0.06
0.03
Concrete Mix Design
In the following the mix designs with varying modiﬁcations to the water absorption coefﬁcient for the F-
RCA are established. The variation of water absorption is chosen as 0%, 12%, and 24% adjustments in the
mix designs. The three different modiﬁed procedures all apply to both F-RCA80 and F-RCA100 leading to
six mixtures named after the concrete mix followed by the corresponding water absorption coefﬁcient. The
notation and the different quantities of each component for each mix design is shown in Table 6.2. For the
full concrete mix design see Appendix E.D.
Table 6.2: Concrete mix designs

All concrete mixes are made with a w/c-ratio of 0.71 aiming for a minimum compressive strength of 20MPa.
Variations in the amount of cement for F-RCA80-0% and the remaining mix designs are due to the mixing
of F-RCA8-0% consisting of 8L where the remaining are corresponding to 7L. During the mixing procedure
additional water is added to the mixtures, the amount is marked with blue in Table 6.2, the additional water
added is further elaborated in relation to the workability of the concrete mixtures.
Particle Size Distribution
The aggregate quantities form Table 6.2 are weighed and sieved in the ﬁne fraction with sieves from 0-8mm,
whereas the coarse fraction is sieved through sieve sizes 4-32mm. The experimental procedure is described
in Annex B, Section 2.1. The particle size distribution for each batch is apparent from Figure 6.1 and 6.2
for the water absorption of 0%, 12%, and 24% for F-RCA80 and F-RCA100 respectively. The ﬁgures are
based on the data found in Appendix E.E - Sieving Results, Table E.E.1 and E.E.2.
12
==PAGE:323==
Figure 6.1: Particle size distribution for the modiﬁed F-RCA80 mixes
From the Figure 6.1 it is apparent that the largest amount of aggregates are found between sieve size 4-
16mm where 60% of the aggregates are retained. Furthermore, it is apparent that the three different batches
have coinciding lines which indicates that the used aggregates are uniformly distributed for each batch.
Figure 6.2: Particle size distribution for the modiﬁed F-RCA100 mixes
The particle size distributions apparent from Figure 6.2 show the same tendency with a slightly better
coherence between the different batches. This ensures that the comparison of compressive strengths will be
determined on the same basis.
13
==PAGE:324==
Workability
The sieved aggregates and the cement is poured into the mixing machine from largest to smallest particle
size, the dry material is mixed for two minutes, after which water is added, and there after the mixing in
continued for further two minutes. During the mixing procedure water is added to the mixtures in case
of dry mixtures in order to obtain a desirable texture of the concrete. For concrete mixture F-RCA80-0%
0.75L are added due to a very dry concrete mix. Whether the concrete is desirable in regard to texture is
determined by conducting slump tests to investigate the workability of the different concrete mixes. The
experimental procedure corresponds to the procedure described in Annex B, section 7.1.
For F-RCA80-12% a rather low slump was observed which led to a remix of the concrete batch with an
addition of 0.25L water, the additional water is written with blue in Table 6.2. The ﬁrst slump test with no
additional water is apparent from Figure 6.3a, where Figure 6.3b shows the slump after adding water and
remixing the batch. The concrete cones for all the modiﬁed F-RCA80 mixtures are apparent from Figure
6.4, whereas the slumps are apparent from Table 6.3.
(a) F-RCA80-12%
(b) F-RCA80-12% after water is added
Figure 6.3: Slump for concrete mix F-RCA80-12% before and after adding water
(a) F-RCA80-0%
(b) F-RCA80-12%
(c) F-RCA80-24%
Figure 6.4: Slump for the modiﬁed F-RCA80 mixes

==PAGE:325==
The slumps found for F-RCA100 are apparent in Figure 6.5, the corresponding slumps are given in Table
6.3.

For the F-RCA100 mixtures solely F-RCA-0% is modiﬁed by adding water to the mixture as apparent from
Table 6.2. Due to limited time mixture F-RCA-12% and F-RCA-24% were not corrected with additional
water which lead to slumps that are lower that optimal prescribed slumps of 60-120mm [1].
6.2
Errors
Errors related to the different tests and determinations are unavoidable, whereas errors in the experimen-
tal procedure, weighing of components, and inconsistencies are present but considered to have a minor
inﬂuence on the results.
6.3
Discussion
The found slumps were lower than the desired values, which is unfortunate seeing that the concrete with
low slumps are hard to work with in practice. A more repetitive procedure where water is added after
each slump test until desired workability is reached would have been preferable to implement to ensure
the applicability of the concrete. Due to lack of time this was not prioritized, instead the water was added
to some mixtures after the slump test as for F-RCA80-12%, in other cases water was added based on the
observed texture of the concrete.
15
==PAGE:326==
6.4
Partial Conclusion
Concrete applicable in practice ought to have slumps of 60-120mm which is not obtained for all mixtures,
however cylinders are casted with the concrete with this kept in mind.
16
==PAGE:327==
7
|
Comparison of Compressive Strengths
In the following the compressive strenghts of the modiﬁed mixing procedure are determined and compared
to the original compressive strengths found for the cylinders tested in Annex B. Two cylinders with a height
of 200mm and a diameter of 100mm are casted from each concrete mix and tested in a uniaxial compression
test after seven days of hardening in water. The experimental procedure is similar to the procedure found in
Annex B, Section 9.1, however in this case no displacements were measured, due to the focus being solely
on the determination of the compressive strengths.
7.1
Compressive Strength
The cylinders dimensions are measured in order to determine the exact compressive strength of each cylin-
der. The dimensions and the compressive strengths are found in Appendix E.F, Table E.F.1 and Table E.F.2,
whereas Table 7.1 shows the mean values of each concrete mix.
Table 7.1: Mean 7 day compressive strength of the modiﬁed F-RCA80 and F-RCA100 mixes
Mix \Water Absorption
0%
12%
24%
F-RCA80 [MPa]
20.5
22.0
18.2
F-RCA100 [MPa]
16.7
22.9
22.0
Table 7.1 above shows that the compressive strength for F-RCA80 is greatest in the mixture where a water
absorption coefﬁcient of 12% is used, the same tendency is apparent for F-RCA100. The lowest strength
is found where the water absorption coefﬁcient is 24% for F-RCA80, whereas the opposite is observed for
F-RCA100 where the lowest strength is found in the mixture with no water adjustments related to water
absorption is made. This makes it difﬁcult to determine the most sufﬁcient water absorption coefﬁcient,
however a beneﬁcial observation is that the compressive strength is beyond the desired 20MPa in most
cases after seven days of hardening.
7.2
Comparison
In order to compare the compressive strengths found for the modiﬁed concrete mixes with the strengths
found in Annex B, which corresponds to the strength after 28 days, the strength development formula is
