In order to illustrate the difference between the estimated strengths and the original compressive strengths,
the strengths are plotted in a bar plot in Figure 7.1 and 7.3 respectively.
Figure 7.1: Original compressive strengths and estimated 28 day strength for F-RCA80
From Figure 7.1 it is apparent that the compressive strength is higher in all the modiﬁed concrete mixes
compared to the originally found strength for the F-RCA80-O mix. An increase of 4.7MPa is found between
the original mix F-RCA80-O and F-RCA80-24% which is comprised of the same concrete mix. The only
difference between the mixtures is found in the water content of the RCA and the mixing machine. This
indicates that the state of the aggregates has a great impact on the compressive strength, and furthermore it
shows that it is possible to reach the desired compressive strength of 20MPa.
However, when seeing the compressive strengths in relation to the slumps, the mixtures that are within the
range corresponding to a applicable concrete are F-RCA80-0% and F-RCA24%. The relation is highlighted
by plotting the slump and the compressive strength of F-RCA80-O and F-RCA80-24% seeing that these
are made on the same basis and thereby most compatible. Figure 7.2 shows the relation between the
compressive strength and the slump. The slump-strength relation for the original F-RCA80 and F-RCA80-
24% is marked with the blue line where the appertaining strength and slumps are (60mm, 22.2MPa) and
19
==PAGE:330==
(150mm, 17.5MPa) for F-RCA-O and F-RCA80-24% respectively. The estimated correlation is plotted as
the grey trend line.
Figure 7.2: Relation between slump and compressive s strength for F-RCA80-O and F-RCA80-24%
The slump-strength relation show that the strength is decreasing as the slump is increasing. If assuming that
the relation is representative for mix F-RCA80 with the water content adjustment of 24% it is apparent that
slumps larger than 100mm will lead to compressive strengths lower than 20MPa. Ideally this relation could
be useful to consider when determining how much water to add to the concrete mix. However, this involves
a larger study and a more comprehensive test series in order to obtain a representative and applicable relation
between slump and strength. Furthermore, this could be expanded to involve the remaining concrete mixes
and thereby form a useful basis for establishing concrete mixtures containing RCA.
The comparison between the original F-RCA100 concrete mix and the estimated compressive strengths for
the modiﬁed concrete mixes are shown in Figure 7.3. For this mixture the original strength is very close to
the strength found for F-RCA100-0% where no correction for water absorption was made, the remaining
mixtures have greater strengths but also lower slumps, which questions the applicability of the concrete
since the mixtures were dry with slumps of 40mm and 20mm for F-RCA100-12% and F-RCA100-24%
respectively.
20
==PAGE:331==
Figure 7.3: Original compressive strengths and estimated 28 day strength for F-RCA100
Overall, the results show that it is possible to reach the desired strength of 20MPa after 28 days for all con-
crete mixes. Furthermore it is observed that the highest compressive strength is found where an adjustment
of water absorption of 12% is made, which applies to both modiﬁed concrete mixes.
21
==PAGE:332==
8
|
Conclusion
The aim of Annex E is to investigate the reasons behind concrete mix F-RCA80 having a signiﬁcantly
lower compressive strength compared to the remaining F-RCA mixes. This is examined by checking for
segregation, performing a remixing procedure, and by investigating the micro-structure of the concrete.
The investigations show that the original concrete was homogeneous with no sign of segregation, and fur-
thermore it is found that the low compressive strength is most likely caused by the large air content in the
mixture. The remixing process underlines the great inﬂuence of the state of the aggregates, seeing that the
concrete turned out to be too wet. This shows that the odd behavior of concrete mix F-RCA80 is due to
a large air content, which was hard to minimize by vibration of the concrete, seeing that the concrete also
contains a large amount of water. This conclusion lead to the investigation of the inﬂuence of adjustments
related to the water absorption coefﬁcient for F-RCA during the establishment of the concrete mixes.
The aim of the modiﬁed mixing procedure is to examine the compressive strengths when varying water
absorption coefﬁcients are considered. Higher strengths are found for the modiﬁed F-RCA80 mixes com-
pared to the original mix, which shows that the low compressive strength of F-RCA80-O is not a general
property but rather related to the water content of the mixes. Furthermore, it is concluded that the origi-
nally determined strength of F-RCA100-O is relatively accurate and not a coincidence seeing that similar
strengths are found when adjusting the water absorption coefﬁcient of the F-RCA. Overall, the modiﬁed
procedure showed that the desirable strength of 20MPa can be obtained regardless of the adjustments of
water absorption. However, this must be set in relation to the slumps, which were rather low in some cases,
especially for the modiﬁed F-RCA100 mixes.
Based on the estimated compressive strengths it is concluded that the greatest compressive strength is found
for the mixes with a water absorption of 12%. This indicates that a beneﬁcial concrete mix design could
be found when an adjustment of water absorption is found between 0% and the determined coefﬁcient,
which is 24% in this study. This underlines the necessity of establishing standards and methods for the
determination of water absorption that are suitable for F-RCA.
Finally, it is concluded that the need for increasing the amount of cement when working with RCA is
disconﬁrmed seeing that the desirable compressive strengths of 20MPa are found for all concrete mixes,
without adding cement.
22
